** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DAVID CASTRO-CAMACHO
File: AXXX XX7 973 - York, PA
March 30, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew J. Mahon, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Keith Hoppes
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Removability; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic and a lawful permanent resident of the United States, has timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s November 21, 2016, decision. During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand the record to the Immigration Judge. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a response to the motion and does not oppose remanding the case to the Immigration Judge. The motion to remand will be granted and the record will be returned to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings with regard to credibility and the likelihood of future events, to determine whether they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Huang v. Att’y Gen. of U.S., 620 F.3d 372, 382-87 (3d Cir. 2010); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review de novo all questions of law, discretion, and judgment and any other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was charged with removability under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), due to his April 9, 2015, conviction for possession of a controlled substance, to wit, synthetic marijuana, in violation of Title 35 of the Pennsylvania Statute, section 780-113(a)(16) (I.J. at 1-2). The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged.
 
The respondent has filed a motion to remand based on the February 6, 2017, decision of the Lancaster County Court of Common Pleas to vacate and dismiss the respondent’s conviction of possession of synthetic marijuana on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent included a copy of the court’s decision with his motion. In its response to the respondent’s motion, the DHS states that it does not oppose the motion. Based on the foregoing, we will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the new evidence in this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992) (holding that motions to remand are subject to the same substantive requirements as motions to reopen); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (a motion to reopen shall not be granted unless it appears that the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing). The Immigration Judge should consider the new evidence and enter a new decision in this case.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1951557 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 1951566 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GABRIEL LOPEZ-SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX4 513 - Miami, FL
March 29, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jack E. Wallace, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Christopher Santora
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before us on August 19, 2016, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 25, 2015, decision ordering the respondent removed from the United States to Mexico. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen proceedings, on November 16, 2016, based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
Due to the issues raised in this motion, we will set forth the procedural history of the case. The respondent was served with a Notice to Appear on April 8, 2013, alleging, inter alia, that the respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico (Exh. 1). The DHS thereafter filed a Form I-213 and a Mexican Consular ID card with the respondent’s name and purported photo. The Form I-213 indicates that the respondent “is a citizen and national of Mexico by virtue of birth on June 28, 1986.” On March 24, 2014, the respondent filed a motion to suppress these documents before the Immigration Judge. Therein, he argued, inter alia, that the DHS’ Form I-213 and ID evidence arose from an illegal arrest, and he sought to terminate proceedings. However, the respondent attached a declaration to his motion which admitted that he was “born in San Geronimo de los Dolores, Mexico, on June 28, 1986.” Thereafter, on April 7, 2014, the respondent filed a motion to retract this declaration, and to substitute a similar declaration without the respondent’s admission of alienage.
 
In a decision dated May 20, 2014, the Immigration Judge found “no just cause” to grant the motion to retract and substitute the respondent’s declarations, and, relying on the respondent’s declaration of alienage, he found the motion to suppress moot (May 20, 2014, I.J. Dec. at 2). The Immigration Judge then shifted the burden to the respondent, under section 291 of the Act, to establish time, place, and manner of entry. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(c). In a pleading dated January 16, 2015, the respondent did not deny his alienage, but also declined to offer evidence on the time, place or manner of his entry. The Immigration Judge ultimately found, in a decision dated February 25, 2015, that the respondent had conceded his alienage and that he had not demonstrated that he had any lawful status in the United States (Feb. 25, 2015, I.J. Dec. at 2-3). Inasmuch as the respondent also did not seek relief from removal, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed to Mexico pursuant to section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act (Feb. 25, 2015, I.J. Dec. at 3). On August 19, 2016, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal, finding that the respondent had not demonstrated an exception to the general rule set forth in Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986), in which we held that an alien’s lawyer’s admissions in immigration proceedings are binding absent “egregious circumstances.”
 
*2 The respondent subsequently filed this ineffective assistance of counsel claim. In support of his motion, the respondent has complied with the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, 18 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Moreover, he argues that the admission of alienage was not a tactical decision, but rather a material “mistake” evidenced by the fact that the basis of his entire motion to suppress involved the suppression of his alienage. He lastly argues that he suffered prejudice due to this mistake because he would have likely had his motion to suppress granted and his proceedings terminated.
 
We disagree. The respondent has cited various cases in which courts have found that nonstrategic admissions of counsel constitute deficient performance. See Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 166 (5th Cir. 2006) (“the effect of admitting the false claim of citizenship charge was to cut off all available avenues of relief”); Hinton v. State, 854 So.2d 254 (Fla.Dist.Ct App. 2003) (finding that when counsel concedes guilt to a charged offense without client’s consent, counsel is presumed ineffective); Nixon v. Singletary, 758 So.2d 618 (Fla. 2000) (holding that counsel’s concession of guilt without defendant’s consent during opening and closing is per se ineffective); Salazar-Gonzalez v. Lynch, 798 F.3d 917 (9th Cir. 2015) (failure to file appeal was not tactical); Benitez-Saldana v. State, 67 So.3d 320 (Fla.Dist.Ct.App. 2011) (where counsel’s factual concessions during opening before jury resulted in conviction, representation was presumed ineffective). We find these cases inapposite to the respondent’s case because they either involve issues in criminal court, or they involve tactical legal issues rather than factual admissions, or both.1 Too many differences in representational rights and standards exist between jury trials in criminal proceedings and the respondent’s immigration proceedings to find these cases persuasive.
 
As such, we find no reason to disturb our previous finding that “no unreasonable professional judgment was involved in the respondent’s admission” (Bd. Dec. at 2). Moreover, we do not find that the respondent has demonstrated the necessary prejudice under Matter of Lozada, supra. While the respondent states, in a conclusory manner, that there is a “reasonable likelihood that the outcome of (the respondent’s) case would have been different” without the concession of alienage, we find this assumption speculative. As we previously noted, neither party has alleged that the respondent’s declaration of alienage is untrue, and insufficient evidence exists to reason that the Immigration Judge might have terminated the respondent’s proceedings absent his admission of alienage. In so finding, we note that the respondent did not proffer evidence once the burden shifted to him to demonstrate the time, place and manner of his entry (Bd. Dec. at 1; Feb. 25, 2015, I.J. Dec. at 2-3). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(c). As such, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Moreover, while the ineffective assistance found in Benitez and Nixon, supra, involved factual concessions, the circumstances involved statements before a jury which were construed as a guilty plea in criminal proceedings.

2017 WL 1951572 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALVARO ARIZMENDI-TRUJILLO A.K.A. ALVARO ARIZMENI
File: AXXX XX7 937 - Kansas City, Missouri
March 28, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
CERTIFICATION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven K. Wichmer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kimberly A. Burgess
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on October 31, 2016, when we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for preparation of a decision pursuant to Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468 (BIA 1999). Previously, the respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeared before an Immigration Judge on September 22, 2015, and agreed to voluntarily depart the United States by January 20, 2016. On December 21, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen which the Immigration Judge denied in an order dated January 21, 2016, from which the respondent appealed. The Immigration Judge prepared a written decision, dated November 22, 2016, and has certified the record back to the Board. The decision of the Immigration Judge will be affirmed.
 
The Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
In his appellate brief, the respondent argues that he is a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, inasmuch as his former counsel allegedly did not advise him of the availability of asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture (Convention) (Resp. Brief at 4-9). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel). However, there is no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s former counsel advised him of the availability of those forms of relief and protection from removal.
 
The record contains a January 12, 2016, letter from the respondent’s former counsel. Therein, counsel states that he discussed with the respondent on November 10, 2014, January 27, 2015, and September 2015, several forms of relief from removal, including whether the respondent fears persecution in Mexico (Letter at 3). According to the letter, the respondent stated that he did not fear persecution in Mexico. Counsel states that he then determined that the respondent did not have a bona fide claim to asylum, and that voluntary departure was his only viable form of relief from removal. In addition, the record contains a September 22, 2015, notice signed by the respondent in which he affirmed that he would not request asylum and understood his obligation to voluntarily depart the United States. The respondent has also not provided sufficient evidence establishing that there is a reasonable possibility that he would be granted relief or protection from removal at a reopened hearing (I.J. at 3). Hence, the respondent has not established that the advice that he received from his former counsel was incorrect. In light of the foregoing, there is no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent knowingly waived his opportunity to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention. It follows that the respondent has not established that he is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 In addition, the respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying reopening of these proceedings based on the respondent’s submission of previously unavailable material evidence, specifically his Form I-589 application for asylum (Resp. Brief at 9-10). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3) (providing that a motion to reopen will not be granted unless the Immigration Judge is satisfied that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). However, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent previously waived the opportunity to file the application, and furthermore, that the proffered application did not present a viable claim for relief.
 
Moreover, the respondent argues on appeal that he established an exceptional situation to reopen these proceedings sua sponte (Resp. Brief at 10). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s and the Immigration Judge’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). He states that he has resided in the United States for a lengthy period, has maintained gainful employment, has a citizen son, and he is afraid to return to Mexico. However, the respondent waived his opportunity to apply for relief from removal at the hearing. Hence, we decline to reopen these proceedings sua sponte.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The decision of the Immigration Judge is affirmed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1951572 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 1951570 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MILTON MODESTO JARAMA
File: AXXX XX6 642 - York, PA
March 28, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaime Jasso, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alice Song Hartye
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s October 20, 2016, decision.1 In that decision, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. The record will be remanded.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he did not knowingly and intelligently waive his right to appeal when his prior counsel accepted pre-conclusion voluntary departure on his behalf. Specifically, the respondent alleges that his prior counsel failed to advise him of his eligibility for adjustment of status.
 
We conclude that remand of the record is warranted. The record reflects that the Immigration Judge never raised the issue of appeal or discussed on the record that the respondent would be waiving his right to appeal by accepting pre-conclusion voluntary departure (Tr. at 58-61). See Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000) (holding that voluntary departure cannot be granted prior to the conclusion of removal proceedings without an express waiver of the right to appeal by the alien or the alien’s representative); see also section 240(c)(5) of the Act (stating that “[i]f the immigration judge decides that the alien is removable and orders the alien to be removed, the judge shall inform the alien of the right to appeal that decision . . . .”). Since the respondent never expressly waived his right to appeal, we conclude that remand of the record is warranted for the Immigration Judge to reassess the respondent’s eligibility for relief. Because we conclude that remand of the record is necessary based on the aforementioned grounds, we need not address whether the respondent established that remand of the record is warranted because his prior counsel engaged in ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and the Immigration Judge’s October 20, 2016, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the respondent’s brief was initially rejected, but his resubmitted brief and motion to remand was accepted on March 13, 2017.


2017 WL 1951570 (BIA)

2017 WL 1508882 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ORRIAN CHRISTOPHER MCGAW
File: AXXX XX2 294 - Buffalo, NY
March 23, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stephen A. Lagana, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brandi M. Lohr
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s March 7, 2016, decision granting him voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) requests that we summarily affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings with regard to credibility and the likelihood of future events, to determine whether they are clearly erroneous. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see Hui Lin Huang v. Holder, 677 F.3d 130, 134 (2d Cir. 2012) (holding that a determination of what will occur in the future and the degree of likelihood of the occurrence is a finding of fact subject only to clear error review); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review de novo all questions of law, discretion, and judgment and any other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On March 7, 2016, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent pre-conclusion voluntary departure and required that he depart the United States by April 6, 2016. On appeal, the respondent claims that he did not knowingly and intelligently waive appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision (Respondent’s Brief at 4). However, his attorney during proceedings before the Immigration Judge expressly waived his right to appeal (Tr. at 22). The respondent claims on appeal that he has never received advice on voluntary departure (Respondent’s Brief at 9). He also claims that if he had known what voluntary departure was, he would have not taken it because it was never his intention to leave the United States (Respondent’s Brief at 9).1 However, the record reveals that the respondent repeatedly testified that he understood he must leave the United States within 30 days, Tr. at 45-47.
 
The respondent was represented by counsel below and the request for voluntary departure and the waiver of appeal made by counsel are binding on the respondent. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal concession made by counsel is binding on the alien); see also Hoodho v. Holder, 558 F.3d 184, 191 (2d Cir. 2009) (holding that facts admitted by a party are “judicial admissions” that bind that party throughout the litigation).
 
*2 Furthermore, to the extent the respondent attributes his lack of knowledge about voluntary departure to his prior counsel, who represented him before the Immigration Judge, the respondent has not meaningfully set forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. In particular, while we recognize that the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit only requires substantial compliance to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), the respondent has not established that he substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, supra. See Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46-47 (2d Cir. 2005).2
 
Inasmuch as the respondent’s attorney expressly waived appeal, the conditions for granting pre-conclusion voluntary departure have been met. See section 240B(a) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D); see also Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000) (holding that pre-conclusion voluntary departure may not be granted prior to the completion of removal proceedings without an express waiver of the right to appeal).3 Therefore, the respondent is not permitted to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision to this Board. Accordingly, we find that this case is not properly before us. The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993).4
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

However, at the hearing, the respondent testified before the Immigration Judge that if the Immigration Judge granted him voluntary departure he had the funds to depart and was willing to do so (Tr. at 29).

2

In order to establish an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must submit the following: (1) an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel did or did not represent in this regard; (2) proof that the former counsel was notified of the allegations of ineffective assistance and allowed an opportunity to respond; and (3) if a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities is claimed, a statement as to whether the alien has filed a complaint with any disciplinary authority regarding counsel’s conduct and, if not, an explanation for not doing so. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

3

The respondent claims in his Notice of Appeal that the Immigration Judge erred by not giving him voluntary departure of 120 days. However, he does not pursue this argument in his brief on appeal. Furthermore, the statute regarding pre-conclusion voluntary departure does not require that the Immigration Judge grant 120 days of voluntary departure. Rather, section 240B(a)(2)(A) of the Act provides that permission to depart voluntarily shall not be valid for a period exceeding 120 days.

4

Therefore, we need not consider the respondent’s request for administrative closure for DHS to consider his request for prosecutorial discretion. Similarly, we cannot consider the respondent’s request for remand. Furthermore, prosecutorial discretion is not within the purview of the Board or Immigration Judge.

2017 WL 1508893 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: INGRID MARISOL PORTILLO-HERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX8 457 - Charlotte, NC
March 22, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Evelyn R.G. Smallwood, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James A. Jones
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 6, 2016, denying her motion to reopen her removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The record will be remanded.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including questions of law and issues of discretion, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent entered the United States as an unaccompanied minor at age 16, and was served with a Notice to Appear shortly thereafter on September 12, 2014. After appearing pro se at her initial hearing, the respondent appeared with prior counsel, Mr. George Miller, at subsequent master calendar hearings. After her former counsel determined that the respondent was ineligible for Special Immigrant Juvenile Status (“SIJS”), he stated that the respondent wished to receive an order of removal. The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed on July 21, 2015. On June 8, 2016, the respondent, who was still represented by Mr. Miller, filed an untimely motion to reopen, arguing that due to a change in law, the respondent was eligible for SIJS, and also that the respondent had a fear of returning to El Salvador and had filed an application for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture before U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”). The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion on June 9, 2016, noting that the motion was untimely and determining that it did not meet the standards for sua sponte reopening. The respondent did not appeal this decision.
 
On August 31, 2016, through new counsel, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen, alleging that proceedings should be reopened due to her prior attorney’s ineffective assistance. Specifically, the respondent argues that prior counsel was unprepared for hearings, failed to inquire into her fear of returning to El Salvador or file an application for relief based on this fear, was unaware of the appropriate jurisdiction in which to file such an application, failed to appeal the Immigration Judge’s removal order, failed to raise arguments to overcome the untimeliness of the June 8 motion to reopen, and failed to appeal the denial of this motion. In support of her motion, she attached, among other evidence, her own and her mother’s statements regarding Mr. Miller’s alleged ineffective assistance, a letter informing Mr. Miller of the allegations against him, a complaint with the state bar association regarding Mr. Miller’s performance, and a declaration containing Mr. Miller’s response to these allegations. The respondent acknowledged that her motion was both untimely and her second such motion, but urged that the time and numerical limitations of section 240(c)(7)(A) and (c)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) and (c)(i), be equitably tolled given prior counsel’s ineffective assistance and her own diligence in discovering the ineffective assistance and filing her motion. See Kuusk v. Holder, 732 F.3d 302, 305-06 (4th Cir. 2013) (recognizing that the principles of equitable tolling apply to untimely motions to reopen removal proceedings).
 
*2 On September 6, 2016, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that it was numerically barred, and again finding that it did not meet the standards for sua sponte reopening. The respondent has timely appealed from that decision.
 
As a threshold matter, we find that the Board has jurisdiction over this matter even though the respondent’s appeal brief reflects that she may no longer be physically present in the United States. See Williams v. Gonzales, 499 F.3d 329, 332-34 (4th Cir. 2007) (invalidating 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d), the regulation treating an alien’s removal from the United States after filing a motion to reopen as withdrawal of the motion); Matter of Armendarez, 24 I&N Dec. 646, 660 (BIA 2008) (acknowledging that the Board will apply the holding in Williams v. Gonzales to cases arising in the Fourth Circuit).
 
The Immigration Judge’s brief decision did not adequately address the issues raised by the respondent in her motion to reopen. See Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994) (holding that the Immigration Judge’s decision must identify and fully explain the reasons for denial of a motion to reopen proceedings). In particular, the Immigration Judge’s decision does not contain any findings of fact or analysis regarding the respondent’s claim that her prior counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel such that the numerical and time limits on motions to reopen are subject to equitable tolling in her case. See Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002) (finding that because the Board’s fact finding ability on appeal is limited, it is important for Immigration Judges to include in their decisions clear and complete findings of fact that are supported by the record and are in compliance with controlling law). We therefore find that the Immigration Judge’s decision is insufficient for our review, and will remand for further consideration of the motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further consideration of the motion to reopen consistent with the foregoing opinion, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Anne J. Greer
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1508893 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 1508901 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARTURO NERI-VELASCO
File: AXXX XX8 038 - Phoenix, AZ
March 20, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher John Stender, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; administrative closure; voluntary departure; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s March 28, 2016, decision denying his motion to continue and alternative motion to administratively close proceedings. The respondent also moves for a remand. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a response. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred by denying his motion for continuance. We disagree. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent asserted this reason for his request for a continuance: that he was dissatisfied with prior counsel and so he wanted a new lawyer to advise him of any available defenses and relief from removal, and have time to make a Freedom of Information Act (“FOIA”) request (I.J. at 2-3; Resp. Motion to Continue). An Immigration Judge “may grant a motion for continuance for good cause shown.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. The respondent has not established that “good cause” existed to further continue these removal proceedings. See id; Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 815 (BIA 2012) (recognizing that a continuance should not be granted where it is being sought “as a dilatory tactic to forestall the conclusion of removal proceedings”).
 
Moreover, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the continuance denial, as he has not shown that he is eligible for any other forms of relief from removal or that any FOIA request made during the pendency of this appeal resulted in a material discovery. See Cinapian v. Holder, 567 F.3d 1067, 1074-75 (9th Cir. 2009). On appeal, the respondent adds that a continuance was necessary to obtain evidence to support his request for prosecutorial discretion from the DHS. However, the respondent did not present this as a basis for the continuance, and moreover, neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over issues of prosecutorial discretion. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-50 (BIA 1982). The respondent also states that a continuance would have helped him obtain evidence to support his motion for administrative closure (resp. Br. at 7-8). However, it is not clear how he would have shown that he warranted administrative closure with no form of relief available to him. In addition, during the pendency of this appeal, the respondent has not submitted any evidence of his DUI conviction being vacated. Therefore, the respondent has not demonstrated that the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny him a continuance was in error or that he experienced prejudice as a result. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-57 (BIA 1983) (noting that an alien must show that the denial of the continuance caused actual prejudice).
 
*2 We agree with the respondent that it appears the Immigration Judge implicitly denied his motion for administrative closure. The Immigration Judge acknowledged the motion in his decision, but ultimately, it is not clear if he explicitly denied the motion (I.J. at 2, 4). Accordingly, the respondent moves for a remand for the Immigration Judge to explicitly adjudicate the motion.1 However, because we have de novo review authority over discretionary issues, such as the motion at issue, we deem it unnecessary to remand to the Immigration Judge for adjudication of the motion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). In addition, our consideration of the motion relies on no new findings of fact. The respondent has not shown that he warrants administrative closure of these proceedings. See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688, 696 (BIA 2012) (outlining factors for evaluating whether administrative closure is warranted, including the likelihood of success on any underlying requests for relief).2 We observe that administrative closure is used to temporarily remove a case from an Immigration Judge’s active calendar or from the Board’s docket. See Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 480 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Avetisyan, supra, at 692. The administrative closure of a case does not result in a final order. It is merely an administrative convenience that allows cases to be removed from the calendar in appropriate situations, subject to being recalendared at a later date. See Matter of Amico, 19 I&N Dec. 652, 654 n.1 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent sought administrative closure to seek prosecutorial discretion from the DHS (Resp. Mot. To Administratively Close Proceedings). However, while the respondent argues that he may have been eligible for prosecutorial discretion, as noted above, the DHS has sole authority to exercise prosecutorial discretion in this matter. See Matter of Quintero, supra. In addition, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent had no apparent relief available to him other than voluntary departure (I.J. at 2). The DHS did not state whether it opposed administrative closure in this case, and it is unknown if the DHS had declined to exercise prosecutorial discretion or has since issued a formal decision on any such request in this matter. Accordingly, we have considered: the reasons for which the respondent sought administrative closure; the unlikelihood that the respondent would succeed on any petition, application, or other action he was pursuing outside of removal proceedings; and the lack of showing that administrative closure would affect the ultimate outcome of these proceedings. Id. at 695. Based on the foregoing and our de novo review of the matter, the discretionary benefit of administrative closure would not have been appropriate in this case. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505 (BIA 1980) (once the DHS institutes proceedings, an Immigration Judge must order an alien deported if the evidence supports the charge); Matter of Avetisyan, supra, at 696. Accordingly, we will deny the respondent’s motion to remand for the Immigration Judge to adjudicate his motion for administrative closure. See, e.g., de Jesus Melendez v. Gonzales, 503 F.3d 1019, 1023 n.1 (9th Cir. 2007) (futile remands are not required).
 
*3 Finally, to the extent the respondent asserts that his due process rights were violated in the proceedings below, we are unpersuaded. See Cruz Rendon v. Holder, 603 F.3d 1104 (9th Cir. 2010) (holding that in order to prevail on a due process claim that she was denied a full and fair removal hearing, an alien must demonstrate that the challenged proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that she was prevented from reasonably presenting her case; and that she suffered prejudice, such that the outcome of the proceeding may have been affected by the alleged violation). The respondent has not established that he suffered prejudice such that the outcome of these proceedings may have been affected by the Immigration Judge’s decision, including the absence of an explicit adjudication of his motion for administrative closure.
 
Moreover, in reference to the respondent’s contentions regarding his former counsel(s), the respondent has also not complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, or demonstrated that he suffered resultant prejudice. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Additionally, because the respondent has not shown that, but for his prior counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, he would have obtained post-conviction relief and been eligible for any other form of relief, i.e., besides voluntary departure, he has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s ineffective assistance. Thus, overall, we conclude that remanded proceedings are not warranted on this basis.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to remand is denied
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. See section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
*4 WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if she provides to the DHS such evidence of her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the respondent has not filed a motion for administrative closure before the Board.

2

In Matter of Avetisyan, the Board held that, in determining whether administrative closure of proceedings is appropriate, an Immigration Judge or the Board should weigh all the relevant factors presented in the case. These factors include, but are not limited to: (1) the reason administrative closure is sought; (2) the basis for any opposition to administrative closure; (3) the likelihood the respondent will succeed on any petition, application, or other action he or she is pursuing outside of removal proceedings; (4) the anticipated duration of the closure; (5) the responsibility of either party, if any, in contributing to any current or anticipated delay; and (6) the ultimate outcome of removal proceedings (for example, termination of the proceedings or entry of a removal order) when the case is recalendared before the Immigration Judge or the appeal is reinstated before the Board. Id. at 695.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAUREEN HOPE RICHARDS
File: AXXX XX0 241 - Pompano Beach, FL
March 20, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bennett Grossman, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider the Board’s May 7, 2013 decision dismissing her untimely and number-barred motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. Any motion to reconsider a decision of the Board must be filed within 30 days after the date of that decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(B). The record reflects that the Board did not receive the motion until January 18, 2017. The motion to reconsider was therefore filed untimely.
 
We also find that the motion does not assert any error of fact or law in the Board’s decision, or argument that was overlooked, but rather seeks to raise additional arguments with regard to the respondents’ removal proceedings and applications for relief. Those issues should have been raised below or on appeal. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006), at 58 (stating that “[a] motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied” and “arguments for consideration on appeal should all be submitted at one time, rather than in piecemeal fashion.”). The respondent argues that she is not removable as charged in the Notice to Appear, specifically that her Grand Theft conviction is neither an aggravated felony nor a crime involving moral turpitude. First, the respondent was only charged with removability under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Act for being convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude within 5 years of admission. Moreover, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged, denied her application for relief, but granted her pre-conclusion voluntary departure. Pursuant to that grant, the respondent waived her right to appeal. As we stated in our prior decisions, the respondent was represented by counsel and has not established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Further, the respondent’s disagreement with our decision is not a basis for reconsideration.
 
We acknowledge the letters of support submitted with the present motion, but they do not establish extraordinary circumstances warranting a sua sponte grant of this untimely motion. For these reasons, the motion fails to meet the regulatory requirements and we see no reason to reconsider the matter sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
*2 Molly Kendall Clark

IN RE: MARCOS CASTILLO-TREJO A.K.A. MARCOS CASTILLO
File: AXXX XX9 569 - Atlanta, GA
March 17, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marsha W. Mignott, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carol D. Riley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, who was previously granted conditional lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s order dated December 6, 2016. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and ordered him removed based on his criminal conviction and termination of his conditional lawful permanent resident status.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
At the hearing, the respondent requested a continuance of proceedings to enable him to pursue post-conviction relief with regard to the 2010 conviction that serves as a basis for removal and renders him ineligible for relief from removal. The respondent alleged that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of this conviction, and he has filed a motion to vacate it (Tr. at 23).2 The Immigration Judge denied the requested continuance (I.J. at 23). On appeal, the respondent challenges denial of the continuance. However, the respondent does not indicate that the conviction has been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009). We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. See also Chaidez v. United States, 568 U.S. __, 133 S.Ct. 1103 (2013) (holding that Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, which requires defense counsel to advise a defendant about the risk of deportation arising from a guilty plea, does not apply retroactively).

IN RE: JOSE JESUS ASTORGA PALOMAR

March 15, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Karla L. Kraus, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
J.L. Woodmansee
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on September 4, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his request for a continuance but granting him the privilege of voluntary departure. On October 12, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the respondent’s motion. The respondent’s motion to reopen will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.§ 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i). The respondent, however, has submitted sufficient evidence to establish that equitable tolling of the filing deadline is warranted in his case. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897-98 (9th Cir. 2003) (stating that equitable tolling of the time limit for a motion to reopen is available “when a petitioner is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the petitioner acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error”). The respondent also has satisfied the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Finally, the respondent has established that he was prejudiced by the errors of his prior attorney. See Matter of Lozada, supra. In particular, the respondent has established that this Board did not reinstate the Immigration Judge’s grant of voluntary departure because the respondent’s prior attorney did not file proof that the respondent had paid the required voluntary departure bond. The respondent, in fact, had paid the bond in a timely manner.
 
In addition, it appears that the respondent might have become eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1), while his case was on appeal to this Board. His motion indicates that his United States citizen daughter was born in December 2013, but his prior attorney did not file a motion to remand to pursue cancellation. The DHS states in its opposition to the current motion that the respondent’s voluntary return to Mexico makes him ineligible for cancellation, but the record does not contain sufficient evidence to establish that any of the respondent’s prior departures constitute breaks in presence. See Matter of Garcia-Ramirez, 26 I&N Dec. 674 (BIA 2015); Matter of Castrejon-Colino, 26 I&N Dec. 667 (BIA 2015). This issue also was not addressed in any detail during the respondent’s hearing before the Immigration Judge, and both the Immigration Judge’s decision and our prior ruling in the respondent’s case were issued before our decisions in Matter of Castrejon-Colino, supra, and Matter of Garcia-Ramirez, supra.
 
*2 Based on the foregoing, we grant the respondent’s motion to reopen and we remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings to determine if the respondent is eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, or, in the alternative, to give him the opportunity to apply for voluntary departure.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order and for the entry of a new decision.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR HUGO TORRES-ORTEGA
File: AXXX XX9 936 - El Paso, TX
March 10, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Saman J. Nasseri, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorely R. Fernandez
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeared before an Immigration Judge on July 8, 2010, and, pursuant to the respondent’s own motion, was ordered removed from the United States. On October 17, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, and appeals from the Immigration Judge’s October 26, 2016, decision denying the motion. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he is a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, inasmuch as his former attorney did not advise him of the availability of voluntary departure. He requests that the Board equitably toll the 90-day period to file his motion, and reopen and remand these proceedings for a voluntary departure hearing.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion. See Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647-48 (5th Cir. 2014) (making mandatory strict compliance with the Lozada requirements and emphasizing that former counsel must be informed of the allegations against them before the allegations are presented in a brief to the Board); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel). Most notably, the respondent has not established that he submitted a complaint against his former counsel with an appropriate disciplinary authority and informed counsel of the allegations. Hence, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. We therefore affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to decline equitable tolling of the 90-day period to file a motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion is therefore untimely.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1330052 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS EDUARDO RAMOS
File: AXXX XX2 535 - Eloy, AZ
March 10, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Juan L. Rocha, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julie Nelson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 22, 2016, denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge denied the motion because it was untimely and the respondent did not establish that he warranted sua sponte reopening.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i). The respondent’s proceedings concluded in December 2011, and he filed the instant motion in July 2016. Thus, the respondent’s motion is untimely by over 4 years. The respondent did not argue that equitable tolling applies as an exception to the filing deadline, and thus, the Immigration Judge did not consider if such exception would apply here. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011) (holding that equitable tolling is available when an individual is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud or error, and exercised due diligence in discovering such circumstances). Thus, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion as untimely filed.
 
We also affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion to reopen sua sponte.1 Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances). The Immigration Judge ruled that the proceedings do not warrant sua sponte reopening based on the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). At the outset, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not establish compliance with the procedural requirements to assert an ineffective assistance of counsel claim pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (I.J. at 4-6).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent contends that he did submit with his motion evidence of his notice to former counsel of his intent to file a motion based on ineffective assistance, as well as former counsel’s response (Resp. Br. at 4). He also argues that he was not required to file a complaint with the state bar or explain his failure to do so, and that his claim was based on his last attorney of record, Mr. Darius Amiri (Id.). The record does not contain the evidence the respondent asserts that he filed with his motion. The respondent’s submission of this evidence with his brief on appeal is insufficient, as he was required to file such proof with his motion before the Immigration Judge. Thus, based upon this lack of proof, the Immigration Judge properly found that respondent did not satisfy the procedural requirements for proving that he was ineffectively represented by his former attorney in these proceedings. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639 (noting that the procedural requirements for filing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim are “necessary if we are to have a basis for assessing the substantial number of claims of ineffective assistance of counsel.... Where essential information is lacking, it is impossible to evaluate the substance of such claim.”).
 
Moreover, the requirement that an ineffective assistance of counsel claim demonstrate the filing of a bar complaint serves to deter meritless claims and highlights the standards that should be expected of attorneys who represent aliens in immigration proceedings. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. It also serves to protect against collusion between alien and counsel in which ““““ineffective assistance” is tolerated, and goes unchallenged by an alien before disciplinary authorities, because it results in a benefit to the alien in that delay can be a desired end, in itself, in immigration proceedings. Matter of Rivera-Claws, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 604 (BIA 1996). The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, does not require strict compliance with Matter of Lozada where “counsel’s ineffective assistance was obvious and undisputed on the face of the record.” Reyes v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1126, 1130 (9th Cir. 2003) (citing Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000)). The respondent’s motion stated that he did file a bar complaint; however, on appeal he appears to state that he did not file a bar complaint because his former counsel’s ineffective assistance is plain on the face of the record, or on the other hand, he was trying to avoid filing a complaint so Mr. Amiri could explain his strategy (Resp. Br. at 7-9). We look to the motion as filed before the Immigration Judge, and note that the respondent did not present to the Immigration Judge proof of filing a bar complaint or Mr. Amiri’s response to his notice, and did not establish that the assistance was ineffective on the face of the record.
 
*3 Moreover, while not considered by the Immigration Judge, we note that the respondent’s motion was not supported by an application for cancellation of removal; and he has not submitted sufficient evidence showing that he has a qualifying relative who might suffer hardship rising to the level of ““““exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” upon his removal. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3) (providing that a motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and “all supporting documentation”). Thus, the respondent did not establish a basis to reopen to apply for cancellation of removal. Likewise, with no accompanying application and supporting documentation, he has not established prejudice from any alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel. See Strickland v. Washington, 466 U.S. 668, 669 (1984) (holding that a showing of prejudice requires that there be “a reasonable probability that, but for counsel’s unprofessional errors, the result of the proceeding would have been different. A reasonable probability is a probability sufficient to undermine confidence in the outcome.”).
 
Considering the totality of the respondent’s circumstances, we are not persuaded to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision. Thus, we concur in the Immigration Judge’s decision to not exercise his sua sponte authority to reopen these proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

It is not evident from the respondent’s motion that he requested sua sponte reopening. However, because the Immigration Judge considered sua sponte reopening, we consider the denial of the motion on this basis, as well.


2017 WL 1330054 (BIA)

2017 WL 1330058 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALEJANDRO GONZALO TREJO
File: AXXX XX5 507 - Charlotte, NC
March 10, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Claudia Canizares, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hilary Rainone
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on April 23, 2014, after not appearing at a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on February 16, 2016, and appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 18, 2016, denying the motion. The appeal will be sustained.
 
The Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that exceptional circumstances, to wit, ineffective assistance of counsel, prevented him from appearing at the hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) (providing that a motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal). The respondent filed his motion nearly two years after the issuance date of his order of removal. He argues that, notwithstanding the untimeliness of the motion, equitable tolling of the 180-day period is warranted. See Kokotis v. U.S. Postal Serv., 223 F.3d 275, 280 (4th Cir. 2000) (equitable tolling is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf); see also Kuusk v. Holder, 732 F.3d 302 (4th Cir. 2013).
 
In support of his request, the respondent argues that his former attorney did not sufficiently notify him of the hearing. He states that he received his order of removal 3 or 4 weeks after the hearing, and he was initially unable to obtain a new attorney. The respondent later filed a bar complaint against his former attorney with the North Carolina Bar Association, and it was rejected because his former attorney was licensed in Florida. The respondent then filed a bar complaint in Florida, to which his former attorney responded on December 3, 2015, and the respondent filed his motion to reopen within a reasonable period thereafter. Based on the totality of the circumstances, we will equitably toll the period for reopening and find that the respondent’s motion is timely filed.
 
In addition, we conclude that the respondent has established that exceptional circumstances prevented his appearance at the hearing. See section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1) (stating that the “[t]erm ‘exceptional circumstances’ refers to exceptional circumstances (such as battery or extreme cruelty to the alien or any child or parent of the alien, serious illness of the alien, or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien”). The respondent’s former counsel was sent notice of the hearing on or about January 15, 2014, and then waited until April 16, 2014, to mail notice to the respondent (Motion at Exhs. C, F). However, the notice was returned to former counsel, possibly because it did not include the respondent’s apartment number (Motion at Exhs. F, H). In addition, the respondent states that he contacted his former counsel’s office three or four times prior to the hearing, and he was not informed of the hearing date during any of those conversations (Motion at H). Former counsel has not responded to this accusation (Motion at C). In light of the foregoing, the respondent has established that exceptional circumstances prevented his appearance at the hearing.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, the in absentia order of removal is vacated, and these proceedings are reopened and remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1330058 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HUMBERTO ABEL RUBI CANALES A.K.A. HUMBERTO RUBICANALES
File: AXXX XX4 093 - Miami, FL
March 9, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fairuze Sofia, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kimberly Hillery
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)] - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
 
APPLICATION: Temporary Protected Status
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s May 24, 2016, decision denying his application for Temporary Protected Status (“TPS”). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a brief opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent conceded that he is removable and sought TPS before the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 3; Exhs. 1, 3). His prior grant of TPS had been withdrawn in 2012 after the respondent did not re-register in 2009 (I.J. at 4; Exh. 2 at 1, 3). It does not appear that the respondent filed an appeal with the Administrative Appeals Unit (see Exh. 2 at 3). The respondent next tried to re-register in 2012 (I.J. at 5; Tr. at 34). The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for relief, finding that he did not establish good cause sufficient to excuse his failure to re-register (I.J. at 5-6). The respondent appeals from that decision.
 
Section 244(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254a(b)(5), sets forth that any administrative procedure for the review of the denial of TPS benefits shall not prevent an alien from asserting TPS protection in removal proceedings if the alien demonstrates that he or she is a national of a state designated under section 244(b)(1) of the Act. The regulations set forth at 8 C.F.R. part 1244, in turn, provide that an alien has the right to a de novo determination of his or her eligibility for TPS before an Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1244.10(d)(1), 1244.11, 1244.18(b). Thus, the respondent is eligible to assert his right to TPS while in removal proceedings. See Matter of Barrientos, 24 I&N Dec. 100, 102 (BIA 2007). He retains this eligibility even though he did not exhaust internal DHS appeal procedures. Matter of Lopez-Aldana, 25 I&N Dec. 49, 51 (BIA 2009).
 
*2 Aliens granted TPS must register annually. See 8 C.F.R. § 1244.17(a). Failure to do so, absent good cause, will cause the withdrawal of TPS. See section 244(c)(3)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1244.14(a)(3), 1244.17(c).
 
In this case, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not establish good cause sufficient to excuse his failure to re-register for TPS. The respondent testified that he did not re-register because he was planning on returning to El Salvador in 2009 (I.J. at 5; Tr. at 34). The respondent testified that he ultimately decided not to travel to that country (I.J. at 5; Tr. at 34, 36). However, at that point, the time for re-registration had passed (I.J. at 5; Tr. at 34). He also contends that he received incorrect advice from a notary regarding the re-registration requirement (I.J. at 4; Tr. at 34). The respondent’s testimony about the notary was brief and without much detail. We are not convinced that the respondent demonstrated good cause, particularly where he conceded that he had re-registered for TPS for several years before 2009 (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 33-34).
 
The respondent in the alternative contends that he received ineffective assistance of counsel when his counsel repeatedly incorrectly indicated that the respondent was ineligible for TPS due to his criminal record (Respondent’s Brief at 5-7; I.J. at 3-4; see, e.g., Tr. at 4-7, 17-18). The respondent further contends that his counsel did not question him regarding his reasons for not re-registering for TPS until the Immigration Judge prompted him to do so (Respondent’s Brief at 6-7; Tr. at 33). We note that in each instance, the Immigration Judge took prompt corrective action. For instance, she noted that the respondent was denied TPS because he did not reregister, rather than because of his criminal history (I.J. at 3-4; Tr. at 17-20). Furthermore, she prompted the respondent’s counsel to question the respondent about his reasons for not reregistering (Tr. at 33). The Immigration Judge’s corrective actions at the very least tend to obviate any prejudice to the respondent. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988) (noting that a successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim requires a showing of prejudice).
 
More fundamentally, the respondent did not comply with the requirements announced in Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent did not submit an affidavit from himself attesting to the relevant facts. Id. at 639. The respondent also has not demonstrated that he informed his counsel of the allegations and provided an opportunity for that counsel to respond. Id. Lastly, the record does not reflect that the respondent filed a complaint with the relevant disciplinary board or otherwise explained why he did not do so. Id. As such, the respondent has not set forth a prima facie claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1330068 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANTIAGO GONZALEZ-ALONZO A.K.A. ALONZO SANTIAGO-GONZALEZ
File: AXXX XX3 832 - Seattle, WA
March 6, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Erin T. Hall, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s March 16, 2016, decision denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings after the issuance of an in absentia order of removal. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal. The appeal will be sustained, the proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.
 
On January 25, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen his removal proceedings following the issuance of an in absentia order on October 20, 2015. Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded upon (1) a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances; or (2) a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he did not receive notice of the hearing, or that the alien was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of his own. See also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999).
 
The respondent alleges that he did not receive notice of his October 20, 2015, hearing because the notice was sent to his former attorney, he had been unable to reach his attorney for some time, and his former attorney did not inform him of his hearing (Respondent’s Brief at 3, 8; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 9). Although the respondent states that he did not receive notice of his October 20, 2015, hearing, he does not base his motion on lack of notice. Rather, he alleges that he did not appear because of exceptional circumstances. Specifically, the respondent alleges that he was unable to reach his prior attorney despite making several attempts, and he eventually retained another attorney to represent him (Respondent’s Brief at 8-9; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 7-8).
 
The record reflects that on October 20, 2015, the respondent’s former attorney attended the respondent’s hearing, and submitted a motion to withdraw as counsel, which was granted by the Immigration Judge. In the motion to withdraw, prior counsel states that she had been unable to communicate with the respondent for several months and was unsure if he still resided at the same address, which was last confirmed in July 2013 (see Administrative Record of Proceedings, Motion to Withdraw as Counsel, dated October 20, 2015). We note that although prior counsel indicates in her motion that a copy of the respondent’s hearing notice was mailed to his last known address, the motion does not include a copy of counsel’s letter or indicate the date that the hearing notice was mailed.
 
*2 The record also reflects that the respondent had timely appeared for his prior hearing, on May 10, 2013. Furthermore, the respondent promptly filed a timely motion to reopen his removal proceedings and to rescind the in absentia removal order. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; see Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 938-39 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that where counsel’s secretary misinformed petitioners of the date of their hearing, causing them to be absent, ineffective assistance of counsel constitutes exceptional circumstances); see also Singh v. Gonzales, 168 F. App’x 800, 801 (9th Cir. 2006) (finding that because petitioner was provided the wrong time of his hearing by his attorney, ineffective assistance of counsel constituted an exceptional circumstance under the circumstances, which warranted reopening).
 
We conclude that the respondent’s actions before and after the entry of the in absentia removal order exhibited a reasonable level of diligence. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 677 (9th Cir. 2011) (stating that due diligence is determined by assessing when a reasonable person in the alien’s position would suspect fraud or error, what steps, if any, the alien took to investigate, and at what point the alien learns of the harm); Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090, 1095-97 (9th Cir. 2007) (requiring due diligence for equitable tolling of the time limitations for motions to reopen). Consequently, under the totality of the circumstances, we conclude that reopening of proceedings is warranted. Thus, the respondent’s appeal will be sustained, and the record of proceeding will be remanded for further proceedings. See Matter of Patel, 16 I&N Dec. 600 (BIA 1978) (stating that a “remand is effective for the stated purpose and for consideration of any and all matters which the immigration judge deems appropriate in the exercise of his administrative discretion”).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The in absentia order of removal is rescinded, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1330137 (BIA)

APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s November 2, 2016, decision finding the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal abandoned and ordering him removed. We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment arising in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo, whereas we review findings of fact in such appeals under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
On appeal counsel alleges that prior counsel provided ineffective assistance to the respondent by failing to file his application for cancellation of removal by the date set by the Immigration Judge, which failure led the Immigration Judge to deem the application abandoned. Counsel has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 1988), in which we set out the procedural requirements for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Respondent’s December 2, 2016, Motion to Remand. We therefore conclude that the respondent has established ineffective assistance below, and we therefore will remand the record to provide the respondent the opportunity to file his relief application with the Immigration Judge.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
