IN RE: ZENADO RUIZ NIEVES A.K.A. ZENAIDO RUIZ NIEVES
File: AXXX XX3 260 - Adelanto, CA
March 2, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven R. Espinoza, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reissuance
 
*1 On July 11, 2016, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s second motion to reissue the Immigration Judge’s December 21, 2015, decision. The respondent has appealed from the denial. The record does not contain a response from the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); see, e.g., Ridore v. Holder, 696 F.3d 907, 915 (9th Cir. 2012) (discussing Board’s scope of review).
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. He was ordered removed by an Immigration Judge in Lancaster, California on November 22, 2000, and was subsequently removed from the United States. On May 31, 2005, an Immigration Judge in Eloy, Arizona, entered a second order of removal against him and he again was removed from the United States. In 2013, the DHS reinstated the 2005 order of removal against the respondent and removed him from the United States for a third time.
 
On December 8, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen his 2000 proceeding.1 The Immigration Judge denied this motion, but the respondent was unable to file an appeal due to errors in the office of his current counsel. The respondent filed a motion to reissue with the Immigration Judge in March 2016 explaining the errors, but the Immigration Judge denied the motion. The respondent filed a second motion to reissue on June 20, 2016. The Immigration Judge again denied the motion, and the respondent now has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision.2
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he had not satisfied the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988), or substantially complied with these requirements. The respondent further claims that he has acted with reasonable diligence and shown prejudice. Accordingly, he maintains that the Immigration Judge should have equitably tolled the time and numeric limits on his motion and granted reissuance. Finally, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge should have granted his motion in the interest of justice pursuant to her sua sponte authority.
 
The Immigration Judge is correct that the respondent’s second motion to reissue is untimely and numerically barred (I.J. at 2). Further, even if the respondent did act diligently in pursuing his motions to reissue and did comply or substantially comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, he has not shown prejudice. See Siong v. INS, 376 F.3d 1030, 1037 (9th Cir. 2004) (noting that prejudice is presumed when counsel’s error deprives alien of appellate proceeding but recognizing that prejudice may be rebutted if there are no plausible grounds for relief). Specifically, the respondent has not established that filing an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s December 21, 2015, decision could change the result in his case. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s ultimate refusal to grant the respondent’s motion to reissue her prior ruling on the basis of the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is correct. See Siong v. INS, supra.
 
*2 For similar reasons, the Immigration Judge’s failure to grant sua sponte reissuance of her decision does not provide a proper basis for sustaining the respondent’s appeal. We therefore dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

1

We note that the respondent has not sought reopening of his 2005 removal proceedings. There also is no indication in the record that these proceedings were consolidated with the 2000 proceedings.

2

In recounting this procedural history in her July 11, 2016, decision, the Immigration Judge made several factual errors. First, the Immigration Judge suggested that her December 21, 2015, decision ordered the respondent removed at the conclusion of proceedings (I.J. at 1-2). The decision, in fact, denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge also stated that the respondent appealed from her denial of his first motion to reissue (I.J. at 1-2). The respondent did not appeal; the respondent instead filed, with the Board, a motion to accept untimely appeal from the Immigration Judge’s December 21, 2015, denial of his motion to reopen. The Board denied this motion on April 27, 2016. The Board did not review the Immigration Judges’ denial of the first motion to reissue. Because the Immigration Judge’s factual errors do not affect the outcome of our decision, however, remand is not required.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CESAR SANTOS-DE PAIVA
File: AXXX XX2 202 - Atlanta, GA
March 1, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Erika I. Goncalves, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 10, 2015. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in not reopening his removal proceedings sua sponte and then administratively closing proceedings, in order that he may seek a provisional unlawful presence waiver and pursue consular processing.1 Upon de novo review, we conclude that the respondent has not established that sua sponte reopening of his proceedings is warranted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). We recognize that, among other equities, the respondent is the beneficiary of an approved visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife. He has a United States citizen child, as well as two stepchildren. We also acknowledge the respondent’s assertion that he failed to appear at his 2006 removal hearing due to the misadvice of an immigration attorney.
 
However, upon consideration of the record as a whole, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has presented insufficient evidence of circumstances that would justify a discretionary grant of his untimely motion to reopen and the extraordinary remedy of reopening his removal proceedings sua sponte. See, e.g.. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (sua sponte reopening is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations); INS v. Rios-Pineda, 471 U.S. 444, 450-51 (1985) (finding that equities which are obtained after the entry of a final deportation order do not create substantial equities). The respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear containing the date and time of his scheduled hearing. Insofar as the respondent asserts that his failure to appear was due to ineffective assistance of counsel, he did not provide sufficient evidence to establish that he hired an attorney or that she filed any application on his behalf. Nor did the respondent comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Finally, we decline the respondent’s request to reopen and administratively close these proceedings. See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 2012).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent makes no claim that his motion to reopen was timely filed or qualifies for an exception to the filing deadline. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).

IN RE: TUM MOM
File: AXXX XX5 071 - Tacoma, WA
February 23, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alexander Y. Chan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s October 26, 2016, decision denying his motion to reopen removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 464-65 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment - and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges - de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s finding that the motion is untimely, where the respondent was ordered removed on May 24, 2012, but did not file the instant motion to reopen until October 3, 2016. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (providing that a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal). We also agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not state any new facts to be proven and did not file any relief applications in conjunction with his motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). In addition, we agree with the Immigration Judge that, to the extent that the respondent was making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, he did not comply with the requirements for making such a claim pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, the record does not show a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002); see also Correa-Rivera v. Holder, 706 F.3d 1128, 1131 (9th Cir. 2013). In fact, the respondent, who was not represented during his removal hearings below, does not refer to any specific attorney in his motion; rather, he claims that he “‘received ineffective advice from another [unnamed] attorney that he not object to entry of his removal order so that he could be released from custody.” Under these circumstances, the respondent did not meet his burden for making such a claim.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1230014 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 1230021 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YAN CARLOS RAMIREZ-CORNEJO A.K.A. YAN CARLOS RAMIREZ A.K.A. YAN CARLOS CORNEJO-RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX3 105 - Florence, AZ
February 22, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher J. Stender, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
David C. Whipple
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On January 22, 2016, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen based on a claimed violation of due process, including ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, now appeals. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings regarding witness credibility and what is likely to happen to the respondent, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Vitug v. Holder, 723 F.3d 1056, 1063 (9th Cir. 2013); Ridore v. Holder, 696 F.3d 907, 915-16 (9th Cir. 2012); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review all other issues, including questions of law, discretion, and judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On March 21, 2006, the respondent was convicted of aggravated assault in Arizona. A Notice to Appear was issued charging the respondent with being inadmissible under sections 212(a)(6)(A)(i) and 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. On August 24, 2006, an Immigration Judge in Eloy, Arizona, granted the respondent’s request for voluntary departure with an alternate order of removal to Mexico. At the time the respondent was represented by attorneys from Stender & Pope (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s Br at 3; Motion at 3).1
 
The respondent re-entered the United States on January 1, 2007. On June 30, 2009, the respondent was convicted of disorderly conduct in Arizona and was sentenced to 1 year of imprisonment. He was issued a Notice to Appear on January 11, 2010.
 
On February 3, 2010, before an Immigration Judge in Florence, Arizona, the respondent admitted the allegations set forth in the Notice to Appear, and conceded the charges of removability. The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent to be removed.
 
On December 30, 2015, the respondent filed a motion to reopen the 2006 voluntary departure order with the Immigration Court in Florence (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s Br. at 3; Motion at 3). The respondent asserts that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel during the 2006 proceedings, because his prior attorney failed to discover the existence of a visa petition that was filed in 1998 by his lawful permanent resident father and approved on October 9, 2002. The respondent also asserts that the government had an obligation to inform him and his father of the visa petition’s approval, but failed to do so. He argues that, at the time of the 2006 proceedings, the approved and then-current visa petition would have provided an opportunity for him to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act as the “child” (at that time) of a lawful permanent resident, in conjunction with a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act. Thus, he asserts that his 2006 decision to accept voluntary departure and waive his appeal rights was not considered and intelligent because his prior counsel and the government did not make him aware of his eligibility for relief (Respondent’s Br. at 6-7, 8, 9-10; Motion at 6-7, 8, 9-10; Attached Exh. K).
 
*2 The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen the 2006 voluntary departure order, because he determined that the motion was not properly before him. The Immigration Judge, citing regulations, determined that the motion should have been filed with the Eloy Immigration Court because that court had created the record of proceedings for the 2006 order and, thus, had administrative control over the record of proceedings (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23; see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(a) (providing that all documents and applications that are to be considered in a proceeding before an Immigration Judge must be filed with the Immigration Court having administrative control over the record of proceedings).
 
The respondent also argues that he has substantially complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (Respondent’s Br. at 11; Motion at 11). Further, the respondent argues that the proceedings in 2010 violated due process because the 2006 proceedings were invalid and because a knowing and voluntary waiver of rights was not obtained. He contends that either the general deadline for seeking reopening should be equitably tolled or proceedings should be reopened sua sponte.
 
Even if the Immigration Judge incorrectly determined that the motion to reopen the 2006 voluntary departure order was not properly before him, the respondent did not demonstrate prejudice for purposes of his due process violation claim, specifically regarding his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Thus, we will affirm the decision to deny the motion to reopen.
 
As stated, the respondent contends that he was not aware of his father’s approved visa petition when he accepted pre-conclusion voluntary departure in 2006. Our review of the 2006 record reflects that there was no discussion of the 2002 approved petition by either party.
 
Nonetheless, we are not persuaded that the respondent’s acceptance of pre-conclusion voluntary departure was not considered and intelligent. The record reflects that the respondent knew about the approved petition filed by his mother; indeed, it was admitted into the record as Exhibit 2. Yet, at the hearing on August 24, 2006, the respondent’s prior counsel informed the Immigration Judge that the parties agreed to pre-conclusion voluntary departure (Tr. dated August 24, 2006, at 33). The respondent informed the Immigration Judge that he accepted the voluntary departure order and waived appeal (DHS Br. in Opposition to Motion to Reopen at 1-3; Tr. dated August 24, 2006, at 34-35). Thus, the record reflects that the respondent’s acceptance of pre-conclusion voluntary departure was considered and intelligent, and his waiver of appeal rights was knowing and voluntary.
 
Based on this information, we conclude that the respondent did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel or prejudice. It appears from the record that the respondent’s decision to accept pre-conclusion voluntary departure was strategic. As the beneficiary of his mother’s approved visa petition, the respondent could have applied for an immigrant visa from the United States consulate in Mexico. Thus, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion to reopen the 2006 voluntary departure order.
 
*3 We next address the Immigration Judge’s denial of the aspect of the motion to reopen relating to the validity of the 2010 proceedings. The Immigration Judge assumed for purposes of analysis that the 2010 proceedings violated the respondent’s due process rights, but ultimately concluded that reopening was not warranted because the respondent was not eligible for the relief sought. Specifically, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent re-entered the United States after his 2006 voluntary departure, had accrued 1 year of unlawful presence before the voluntary departure,2 and was “““seeking admission” via the adjustment of status application. The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent therefore was inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act, and thus, was not eligible for the relief sought, i.e., adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act (I.J. at 3).
 
We held in Matter of Lemus-Losa, 25 I&N Dec. 734 (BIA 2012), that adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act is unavailable to an alien who is inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act, absent a waiver. See id. at 734, 744-45. The respondent did not request a waiver under section 212(a)(9)(B)(v) of the Act in conjunction with his motion to reopen. Indeed, the respondent did not address on appeal the Immigration Judge’s determination that he was inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act, and thus, was not eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act. We discern no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion to reopen with respect to the 2010 removal order.
 
Finally, inasmuch as the respondent did not apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(9)(B)(v) of the Act, and, is not otherwise eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act based on his inadmissibility under section 212(a)(9)(B)(i)(II) of the Act, there is no basis to reopen sua sponte either by the Immigration Judge or the Board (Respondent’s Br. at 16-17; Motion at 16-17).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

The record reflects that the respondent’s current attorney, Mr. Stender, was one of the respondent’s attorneys during the 2006 proceeding, as evidenced by the Forms EOIR-28 (Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative before the Immigration Court) dated June 20, 2006, and July 9, 2006.

2

The Immigration Judge found, in pertinent part, that before the respondent’s 2006 voluntary departure, he was unlawfully present in the United States for approximately 1 year and 8 months after reaching the age of 18 years (I.J. at 3).

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANA ALVIZURES PEREZ A.K.A. ANA ALVIZURES
File: AXXX XX1 765 - Boston, MA
February 16, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey Rubin, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals an Immigration Judge’s August 11, 2016, order denying her second untimely motion to reopen an in absentia order of removal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review issues of law, discretion, or judgment de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent argues that her proceedings should be reopened and the in absentia order rescinded because her previous counsel provided ineffective representation, by failing to calendar the hearing in his book or to inform the respondent about it, and then by attempting to file two motions to reopen that were procedurally defective and consequently rejected. Also, the respondent maintains that exceptional circumstances prevented her from earlier seeking to reopen proceedings, in that she did not have the necessary funds to hire another attorney and she was caring for her pregnant daughter with special needs.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision that the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 180 days of the in absentia order that had been entered on March 2, 2012. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). It is also number-barred, as it is the second motion to reopen filed in this case. Section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
Also, even if we were to consider the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we would nonetheless affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen and to rescind the in absentia order, as the respondent failed to show that she exercised due diligence in pursuing reopening of her removal proceedings. The respondent’s contention that we should reopen proceedings sua sponte because she lacked the financial means to secure another attorney after the entry of her in absentia order, and was devoted to the care of her pregnant daughter with special needs, presents sympathetic circumstances but is not sufficient to excuse the nearly 4-year delay in the submission (on March 2, 2016) of her initial properly filed motion to reopen the March 2, 2012, in absentia order, or to persuade us that she was duly diligent in seeking reopening. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 We recognize that the respondent asserted in the declaration accompanying her most recent motion to reopen that she did not even learn that she had a removal order until she sought the assistance of her current counsel (Exh. J to Motion filed July 22, 2016). However, in a letter to the Office of the Bar Counsel in Massachusetts in response to a complaint filed by the respondent, her former counsel states - and the respondent apparently has not disputed - that he forwarded the respondent’s file to her current counsel’s office in August of 2013 (Exh. N to Motion filed July 22, 2016). Thus, even if the time that elapsed before the respondent consulted her current counsel is not taken into account, or is viewed as tolled, the respondent still waited over 2½ years after that consultation before filing her March 2, 2016, motion to reopen - more than 2 years beyond the normal time limit even after equitable tolling. See Neves v. Holder, 613 F.3d 30 (1st Cir. 2010) (generally, a litigant seeking equitable tolling bears the burden of establishing two elements: (1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way); Bead v. Holder, 703 F.3d 591 (1st Cir. 2013). We do not conclude that the circumstances presented by the respondent suffice to justify such a lengthy delay, and we therefore will decline to reopen proceedings on the basis of either equitable tolling or our sua sponte authority.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: EDIS CELENIA GODOY-JIMENEZ KARLA SUYAPA GODOY-JIMENEZ KIANA CELENIA HERNANDEZ-GODOY
Files: AXXX XX1 085 - Charlotte, NC1 AXXX XXX XXX AXXX XXX XXX
February 14, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Daniel W. Thomann, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scott D. Criss
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondents, natives and citizens of Honduras, have filed an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 18, 2016, denying their request for a continuance and deeming their applications for relief abandoned. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a motion opposing the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondents failed to establish good cause for a continuance in their case (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). The Immigration Judge noted below that counsel made only a verbal request for a continuance with no supporting documentary evidence (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 20-22, 24).2 Compare Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009) (setting forth factors to consider when adjudicating a motion for a continuance based on a pending visa petition, noting that focus should be on ultimate likelihood of success on adjustment application). On appeal, counsel argues that the respondents were denied due process. However, to date counsel has still not submitted evidence that he timely mailed the respondents’ asylum application (Form I-589) to the court, and it is not contained in the record before us. We conclude that the respondents have failed to exercise due diligence in pursuing any claims to relief. See Matter of Sibrun, supra, at 356.
 
The denial of a continuance will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing, and here the respondents have not shown such (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 1-25). See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987). Furthermore, the respondents have failed to show prejudice. See Matter of Sibrun, supra, at 356-58. As noted above, to date they have not shown the pendency of any applications for relief. Id. Finally, we are not persuaded that sua sponte reopening would be appropriate in this case, where they have failed to establish due diligence in pursuing relief from removal. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (party seeking reopening bears heavy burden); see also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (requirements for filing motion to reopen based on claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
*2 We also agree with the Immigration Judge deeming all applications for relief abandoned (I.J. at 2). A potential applicant must file all application forms on or before a filing deadline set by the Immigration Court. See Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). The respondents were represented by the same attorney below and on appeal, who was informed of the filing deadline of July 18, 2016 on the record (I.J. at 1-2; Tr. at 17). To date, counsel for the respondents has not proffered a complete Form I-589 or supporting documents, and has not submitted evidence that the application was timely filed.3
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant

As noted above, the record before us does not contain a Form I-589 for the respondents, nor does it contain proof of timely mailing as claimed by counsel. The lead respondent stated on the record that she understood the Immigration Judge’s decision deeming all applications as abandoned due to failure to file by the court’s deadline (Tr. at 24).

IN RE: OMAR FEDERICO MIRANDA-DE HARO
File: AXXX XX0 209 - Atlanta, GA
February 13, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcelle Anne Rice, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Andrew J. Hewitt
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. On January 22, 2001, the Board summarily affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge. Over 15 years later, on November 4, 2016, the respondent filed the current motion to reopen. The motion to reopen is untimely. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent alleges that proceedings should be reopened and remanded due to ineffective assistance of counsel. Alternatively, the respondent seeks sua sponte reopening or reissuance of our January 22, 2001, decision. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes reopening. The motion is denied.
 
Equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel generally requires a litigant to show (1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way. Avila-Santoyo v. Atty Gen., 713 F.3d. 1357, 1363 n. 5 (11th Cir. 2013). The respondent’s former attorney has been disbarred. The respondent claims that his former attorney was ineffective because she did not file an appeal brief on his behalf, and, therefore, the Board summarily dismissed his appeal. This argument is inaccurate because the Board summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision under former 8 C.F.R. § 3.1(a)(7).1 See Mendoza v. Atty Gen., 327 F.3d 1283, 1288-89 (11th Cir. 2003).
 
The respondent also asserts that under the circumstances, he acted diligently in filing the present motion (Motion at p. 15). The respondent claims that he consulted with a law firm in 2011, but he did not pursue his rights because he was ashamed, embarrassed and discouraged (Motion, Exh. B). The respondent’s explanation is insufficient to show either that he has been pursuing his rights diligently since his appeal was dismissed in 2001, or that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way since that time. During the intervening years, the DHS deported him several times (DHS Opposition, Exhs. A-D), which placed the respondent on notice, but he still did not pursue his immigration rights diligently. As such, we find that equitable tolling does not apply here.
 
The respondent also seeks sua sponte reopening. He argues that he is no longer removable as charged, and that his family would be upset if he were deported. However, the respondent was properly removable as charged in 2001, and his reliance on precedent from the Ninth Circuit is not binding on the Board as this proceeding is governed by Eleventh Circuit precedent. Moreover, although it appears that he voluntarily departed during the pendency of his appeal (DHS Opposition, Exh. A), the Board considered his appeal, and summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision. Further, the respondent was removed on at least one other occasion during the intervening years (DHS Opposition, Exh. D). Accordingly, we decline to exercise our sua sponte discretionary authority to reopen proceedings because the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances justifying the exercise of our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
*2 Last, the respondent requests that we reissue our January 22, 2001, decision so that he may file a petition for review with the Eleventh Circuit (Motion, p. 19). We decline to reissue our January 22, 2001, because the respondent has not demonstrated due diligence. Although not argued by respondent, we note that he has highlighted certain items in his exhibits as if to imply that the Board erroneously mailed our decision to the physical location of his former attorney’s office, as opposed to a P.O. Box (Motion, Exh. W). The record of proceeding indicates that the decision was properly mailed to the address listed on the Notice of Entry of Appearance filed by the respondent’s former attorney, and there is no indication that our decision was returned as undeliverable. See Motion, Exh. W. Last, due to the respondent’s interactions with DHS since issuance of the 2001 decision, he was on notice that a final Board decision had been issued in this case. For the forgoing reasons, the motion will be denied.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

Section 3.1(a)(7) has been redesignated 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4).

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DORILA MEDRANO-HENRIQUEZ
File: AXXX XX5 937 - Dallas, TX
February 13, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aaron Chenault, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s August 8, 2016, decision denying her motion to reopen her removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the appeal, which will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear (NTA) was served personally on the respondent on January 22, 2016 (Exh. 1). At a hearing on February 29, 2016, the Immigration Judge informed the respondent and her attorney that her failure to appear for her next scheduled hearing and to meet the filing deadline for her application for relief would result in an order of removal (I.J. at 2). On March 8, 2016, the respondent filed a motion for a change of venue. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion and specifically noted that the application for relief was still due by March 10, 2016. Id. As the respondent did not appear for her hearing on March 10, 2016, and did not file an application for relief, the Immigration Judge ordered that all claims for relief were deemed abandoned and ordered the respondent removed. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c).
 
On July 26, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The respondent argued that exceptional circumstances caused her failure to appear for her hearing, as she did not believe she was required to appear for it or that she was required to submit her application by that date due to her release from immigration custody (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had not demonstrated that exceptional circumstances caused her to miss her filing deadline and that the record did not warrant sua sponte reopening (I.J. at 3).1
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. A failure to request relief in accordance with a schedule set by the Immigration Judge constitutes a waiver of the opportunity to file for such relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (stating that if an application is not filed within the time limit set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application shall be deemed abandoned). The Board has long held that applications for benefits under the Act are properly denied as abandoned when the alien fails to timely file them. See Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); Matter of Jean, 17 I&N Dec. 100, 102 (BIA 1979).
 
*2 The administrative record reflects that the Immigration Judge informed both the respondent and her attorney that she must file the application for relief by her next hearing on March 10, 2016, or her application for relief would be deemed abandoned and she would be ordered removed (I.J. at 2). See Respondent’s Motion to Change Venue at 1. The respondent and her attorney did not file any applications for relief by the filing deadline. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent’s claims for relief were deemed abandoned.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent and her counsel’s “““purported ignorance” do not warrant reopening of her proceedings (I.J. at 2). The respondent argued that her counsel’s clerk informed her that she did not need to appear for her March 10, 2016, hearing, and that she was unaware she was required to adhere to a filing deadline. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2-3. The respondent’s counsel argued that he understood the Immigration Judge’s instructions to mean that the respondent must adhere to the filing deadline if she were still detained and on the detained docket. Id. at 6.
 
An attorney’s administrative error is not a basis for reopening absent compliance with the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998); see also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647-48 (5th Cir. 2014) (making mandatory strict compliance with the Lozada requirements and emphasizing that former counsel must be informed of the allegations against them before the allegations are presented in a brief to the Board). The respondent has not established ineffective assistance of counsel for her failure to timely file her applications for relief. The respondent has not submitted an affidavit setting forth the facts and her agreement with her counsel, nor has she provided any evidence that her counsel has been made aware of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond, either after or before she filed her brief to the Board, nor did she indicate that a disciplinary complaint had been filed against that attorney. See Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, supra, at 647. Thus, reopening is not warranted in this matter, as the respondent has not complied with the requirements set forth in Lozada. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In his decision dated March 10, 2016, the Immigration Judge did not issue an in absentia order of removal. As the Immigration Judge did not actually order the respondent removed in absentia, the respondent’s motion to reopen on the basis of exceptional circumstances for failure to appear is inapplicable. Moreover, in a deportation case pursuant to section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b), the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit held that where an alien failed to appear for a hearing and the Immigration Judge issued an order stating that all claims for relief were deemed abandoned, the hearing was not held in absentia. See Williams-Igwonobe v. Gonzales, 437 F.3d 453 (5th Cir. 2006).

2017 WL 1230073 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE GUERRERO-SOTO
File: AXXX XX5 150 - Houston, TX
February 9, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board to reconsider its September 22, 2016, decision dismissing his appeal from the Immigration Judge’s January 13, 2016, decision denying his untimely motion to reopen. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57-58 (BIA 2006); section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent was found removable as charged on November 17, 2005, and removed thereafter to Mexico. The respondent then returned to the United States, and was removed a second time in September 2012. On December 18, 2014, the respondent moved to reopen the removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent asserted that pursuant to the Supreme Court’s decision in Lopez v. Gonzales, 549 U.S. 47 (2006), his conviction for possession of a controlled substance was no longer considered an aggravated felony, thus rendering him eligible to apply for cancellation of removal. He further asserted that he was not precluded from filing a motion to reopen his removal proceedings because the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit (“circuit court”) had abrogated the departure bars at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(d) and 1003.23(b)(1). See Garcia-Carias v. Holder, 697 F.3d 257 (5th Cir. 2012). In a decision dated February 4, 2015, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely, and the respondent appealed.
 
On November 17, 2015, the Board remanded the respondent’s case to the Immigration Judge to consider whether equitable tolling of the filing deadline applied to the respondent’s motion to reopen. In remanded proceedings, the respondent argued that equitable tolling should apply because he did not learn of the decision in Garcia-Carias v. Holder, supra, until late in 2014. On January 13, 2016, the Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely, and on September 22, 2016, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from that decision.1 The respondent now seeks our reconsideration.
 
The dispositive issue before the Board on appeal was whether the Immigration Judge properly determined that the equitable tolling of the filing deadline imposed on the motion to reopen was not warranted. In our decision, we found no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent’s attorney filed a Notice of Entry of Appearance (Form EOIR-28) on the respondent’s behalf on October 22, 2012, and we agreed with the conclusion that the respondent did not demonstrate his diligence or an extraordinary circumstance following the issuance of Garcia-Carias v. Holder, supra, on September 27, 2012, to warrant equitable tolling. See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337 (5th Cir. 2016). In the instant motion, the respondent asserts that he was unaware of his attorney’s representation at that time. The respondent’s assertion, however, does not alter the fact that he was represented by counsel in October 22, 2012, and that the motion to reopen was not filed until December 18, 2014. Further, the respondent has not raised any allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 The respondent also contests the Board’s finding that his contentions on appeal regarding the fairness of the proceedings before the Immigration Judge lacked merit. The respondent argued on appeal that the Immigration Judge acted in a biased and impartial manner because he admonished the respondent’s attorney for failing to disclose information pertinent to the respondent’s second removal from the United States. In this motion to reconsider, the respondent argues that such information was irrelevant to the issue of equitable tolling. The respondent, however, has offered no persuasive argument that the Immigration Judge’s criticism of the respondent’s attorney prejudiced the outcome of this case in any way. See generally Matter of Exame, 18 I&N Dec. 303 (BIA 1982).
 
In sum, we find no error of fact or law in our prior order to warrant reconsideration.2 See Matter of O-S-G-, supra. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s petition for judicial review of the Board’s September 22, 2016, decision is currently pending. Guerrero-Soto v. Lynch, No. 16-60693 (5th Cir., filed 19 Oct. 2016).

2

To the extent that the respondent argues that the Board and the Immigration Judge erred in not reopening the proceedings sua sponte, we emphasize that the circuit court has upheld the application of the departure bar to untimely motions to reopen removal proceedings. See Ovalles v. Holder, 577 F.3d 288, 296-98 (5th Cir. 2009); Navarro-Miranda v. Ashcroft, 330 F.3d 672, 675-76 (5th Cir. 2003). This Board and the Immigration Judge thus lack jurisdiction to reopen the respondent’s removal proceedings sua sponte in light of the departure bars at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(d) and § 1003.23(b)(1).

2017 WL 1230076 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PERFECTO ALATORRE-FRAUSTO A.K.A. PERFECTO FRAUSTO ALATORRE A.K.A. PERFECTO FRAUSTO A.K.A. PERFECTO ALATORE-FRAUSTO
File: AXXX XX5 428 - Eloy, AZ
February 8, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murray David Hilts, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Elly Laff
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 26, 2016, denying his motion to reopen the instant removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal, which will be dismissed.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent contended that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel by the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 3-4; Mot. dated May 24, 2016, at 4-8). Specifically, the respondent alleged that he was pro se in the prior proceedings and would have applied for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a),1 but for the Immigration Judge’s erroneous advisal that his conviction for possession of a controlled substance, to wit: marijuana, in violation of CAL. HEALTH & SAFETY CODE § 11350(a), was for an aggravated felony, and thereby rendered him ineligible for any form of relief from removal. He further argued that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled because he did not learn of the error until 2015, after he consulted with present counsel. The respondent further argued in the alternative that the proceedings should be reopened in the exercise of the Immigration Judge’s sua sponte authority.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the motion, holding that it was untimely filed and was not subject to the one of the regulatory exceptions to the filing deadline (I.J. at 4) and, moreover, that the respondent did not establish that a due process violation warranted reopening (I.J. at 4-5). The Immigration Judge also declined to reopen the proceedings sua sponte (I.J. at 5-7).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he was properly advised of his eligibility for relief based on the law at the time of the hearing (Resp’t Br. at 5-10). On this point, he argues that the Immigration Judge conducting the 2004 hearing should have found that the respondent’s state crime did not fall under the first or the second prong of the definition of aggravated felony set forth in section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B). The respondent further argues that the Immigration Judge erred in refusing to toll the filing deadline for his motion, asserting that the Immigration Judge considered tolling in the context of considering reopening in the exercise of his sua sponte authority but not on the basis of the ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and overlooked the equities in the record (Resp’t Br. at 2-3, 10-13). The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge should have reopened the proceedings in the exercise of his sua sponte authority (Resp’t Br. at 13-14).
 
*2 On review, we are not persuaded that the Immigration Judge’s decision should be reversed. First, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the motion was untimely filed, see section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), and that the motion did not fall within the statutory or regulatory exceptions to the motions time limits in removal proceedings, see sections 240(c)(7)(C)(ii)-(iv) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(ii)-(iv); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4).
 
Second, while an Immigration Judge has a duty “to inform an alien of “‘apparent eligibility’ for relief,” see, e.g., United States v. Lopez-Velasquez, 629 F.3d 894, 896-97 (9th Cir. 2010) (en banc) (quoting 8 C.F.R. § 1240.11(a)(2)), the respondent has not cited any authority supporting his contention that an Immigration Judge performing this duty is to be regarded as the respondent’s attorney, and that a deficiency in the performance of this duty is redressed by a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We are thus not persuaded by the respondent’s appellate contention that the Immigration Judge rendered ineffective assistance of counsel and that the motions time limits may be equitably tolled consistent with the standards applicable to motions premised on a claim to ineffective assistance of counsel. Rather, we agree with the Immigration Judge that such a claim sounds in due process, and that the respondent has not established that he was denied due process of law.
 
Contrary to the respondent’s appellate contentions, we find that the Immigration Judge’s actions were consistent with a fair hearing, and find no support for the respondent’s claims as to a denial of due process. The Immigration Judge in 2004 evaluated the relief available under the law then applicable, which did not establish the respondent’s apparent eligibility for relief. See Valencia v. Mukasey, 548 F.3d 1261 (9th Cir. 2008) (alien lacking apparent eligibility for relief from removal did not have due process right to notice of such relief). As DHS contended below, the Immigration Judge reasonably explained to the respondent that he did not have an opportunity to apply for relief because his conviction was considered to be for an aggravated felony under then-current law (DHS Opp. dated June 7, 2016, at 6), and the respondent knowingly and voluntarily accepted his removal order and waived his right to appeal (I.J. at 5).
 
Moreover, even assuming the respondent was apparently eligible for cancellation of removal in 2004, he has not carried his burden of showing that he was prejudiced by the violation because he has not made a “‘plausible showing’ that an IJ presented with all of the facts would exercise discretion in the alien’s favor.” See United States v. Gonzalez-Flores, 804 F.3d 920 (9th Cir. 2015) (citing United States v. Rojas-Pedroza, 716 F.3d 1253, 1263-64 (9th Cir. 2013)).
 
*3 We are also not persuaded that the proceeding should be reopened in the exercise of sua sponte authority. The Immigration Judge acknowledged that the subsequent decisions in Moncrieffe v. Holder, 133 S. Ct. 1678 (2013), and Lopez v. Gonzales, 549 U.S. 47 (2006), holding that simple possession convictions were not felonies under the federal Controlled Substances Act, could be a fundamental change in law, see Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec, 1132 (BIA 1999), but he concluded that the respondent did not demonstrate that he acted with due diligence after the decisions were issued, see Matter of G-C-L-, 23 I&N Dec. 359 (BIA 2002) (setting forth reasons for no longer sua sponte reopening cases a reasonable period of time had passed from fundamental change in law). On this point, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent waited 3 years after the decision in Moncrieffe, and 10 years after the decision in Lopez, to file his motion to reopen, and he did not demonstrate why he waited 12 years before having counsel review his removal proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed. The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent submitted an application for cancellation of removal in support of the motion (I.J. at 4; Mot. dated May 24, 2016, Attachment C).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEYED TOFIGH MIR HADIAN A.K.A. SEYED MIR HADIAN
File: AXXX XX8 601 - San Francisco, CA
February 8, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Monica N. Ganjoo, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)] - Conditional resident status terminated
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Iran, has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated May 23, 2016, denying his motion to reopen the proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against him on February 22, 2011. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including any determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The respondent received proper notice of his removal hearing because he was personally served with notice of the time, date, and place of the hearing. Section 239(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a). The respondent’s motion is untimely with respect to a claim of exceptional circumstances because he did not file his motion within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s decision. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge properly declined to equitably toll the applicable filing requirements based on the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The doctrine of equitable estoppel applies when “despite all due diligence, [the party invoking equitable tolling] is unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of the claim.” Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001). Here, the respondent had been put on notice that he was in removal proceedings and was scheduled to appear for a hearing on February 22, 2011. Although the respondent states that former counsel did not inform him of the consequences of failure to attend the hearing, the respondent was informed of such both by the Notice to Appear which had been served on him by regular mail at his home address and by the Notice of Hearing. Sections 239(a)(1)(G)(ii) and 239(a)(2)(ii) of the Act.
 
*2 Additionally, he does not claim that former counsel instructed him not to appear for the hearing. Rather, he asserts that prior to the hearing, he had a falling out with counsel after discovering counsel had been suspended from practice, and he did not want to attend the hearing without representation. The respondent’s decision not to attend a scheduled hearing does not constitute an exceptional circumstance, nor is equitable tolling of the deadline warranted where the respondent has not shown that he was “unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of the claim.” Id. Finally, the respondent has not presented an “exceptional” situation sufficient to warrant sua sponte reopening of the proceedings. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD

2017 WL 1130641 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DAVINDER SINGH A.K.A. DAVINDER THAPA
File: AXXX XX3 371 - Harlingen, TX
February 6, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Suraj R. Singh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rod Wolthoff
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s May 26, 2016, decision denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings after the issuance of an in absentia order of removal. The respondent’s appeal, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security, will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear (NTA) was served personally on the respondent on February 16, 2016 (Exh. 1). A Notice of Hearing (NOH) dated March 11, 2016, was sent to the respondent’s former counsel scheduling a hearing for April 6, 2016. As the respondent did not appear for his hearing on April 6, 2016, the Immigration Judge ordered him removed in absentia.
 
On May 9, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, alleging lack of notice of his April 6, 2016, hearing. The respondent claimed that he did not receive an NOH and that his prior attorney told him that his case would automatically change venue from Harlingen, Texas, to New York, New York, upon the respondent’s release from immigration custody (I.J. at unnumbered 1). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent received constructive notice of the hearing, as the record showed that the Immigration Court mailed the respondent’s prior attorney an NOH informing him of his April 6, 2016, hearing. Id.
 
The respondent filed his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision on July 19, 2016. On appeal, through new counsel, the respondent admits that he was aware of his scheduled hearing date of April 6, 2016, but asserts that the Immigration Judge did not properly consider his claim that his former counsel gave him wrong advice not to appear for that hearing. See Respondent’s Brief at unnumbered 3-4. In addition, the respondent alleges that his prior attorney’s misleading advice constituted exceptional circumstances that caused his failure to appear. See Respondent’s Brief at unnumbered 4.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. As the respondent’s attorney received notice of the hearing, we agree with the Immigration Judge that proper notice was provided to the respondent. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). The respondent did not establish that he failed to appear for the hearing due to “““exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). The Board has held that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the respondent of his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988); Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986).
 
*2 We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “““exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996).
 
An attorney’s administrative error or his failure to advise a client that a change of venue motion had not been granted and that he must appear for the hearing is not a basis for reopening absent compliance with Lozada; see also Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647-48 (5th Cir. 2014) (making mandatory strict compliance with the Lozada requirements and emphasizing that former counsel must be informed of the allegations against them before the allegations are presented in a brief to the Board). We are not persuaded by the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge erred in not properly considering his claim of ineffective assistance concerning his former counsel. The respondent has not established ineffective assistance of counsel for his failure to appear. The respondent has not submitted an affidavit describing the facts and his agreement with his former counsel, nor has he provided any evidence that his former counsel has been made aware of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond, either after or before he filed his brief to the Board, nor did he indicate that a disciplinary complaint had been filed against his prior attorney. See id. Thus, reopening is not warranted in this matter, as the respondent has made no attempt to comply with the requirements set forth in Lozada. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1130641 (BIA)

APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was last before us on September 14, 2016, when we dismissed as untimely the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen. The respondent now seeks reconsideration of that decision. The respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
In his motion, the respondent asserts that he was experiencing extreme financial hardship when his appeal was due and that he did not understand the consequences of failing to appeal in a timely manner. The respondent raised the claim of financial hardship when he submitted his appeal, and we considered that argument at that time. Further, the respondent appears to have been represented by counsel both in the filing of his motion to reopen and in filing an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of that motion. The respondent has not made a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and he has not explained why he was unable to file a fee waiver request with a timely appeal.
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent has not established that reconsideration of our decision dated September 14, 2016, is warranted. We therefore deny his motion to reconsider.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.

2017 WL 1130673 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHRISTIANH OLUBUNMI SUTTON
File: AXXX XX1 670 - Las Vegas, NV
January 30, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
J. Oscar Shaw, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Petition to remove conditional basis of permanent residence
 
*1 This case is before us pursuant to an order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit dated August 2, 2016, which denied the petition for review in part but remanded for the Board “to address [the petitioner’s] contention that her former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to pursue a hardship waiver.” We will again dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In our decision dated October 10, 2014, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent relief from removal. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent was not credible and that she had not provided sufficient evidence to meet her burden of establishing that she entered into a “““good faith marriage” for purposes of a waiver under section 216(c)(4)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(4)(B) (I.J. at 2-4). The Ninth Circuit dismissed the petition for review insofar as it challenged those findings. However, the Ninth Circuit remanded on the issue of whether the respondent’s prior counsel provided ineffective assistance because she did not file for a hardship waiver under section 216(c)(4)(A) of the Act.
 
We conclude that the respondent did not establish that her former counsel provided ineffective assistance. Even assuming that failure to file an application for a waiver before the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services could constitute ineffective assistance of counsel in removal proceedings, the respondent has not presented evidence that she substantially complied with the procedural requirements for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); United States v. Lopez-Chavez, 757 F.3d 1033, 1044 (9th Cir. 2014); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 213 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). The respondent’s mere assertion of what action her former counsel should have taken is not sufficient to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel. Id.
 
*2 Additionally, the respondent has not demonstrated that she suffered any prejudice as a result of the purported ineffective assistance of her former counsel. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that despite a finding of ineffective assistance of counsel, prejudice had not been demonstrated and relief was correctly denied); United States v. Lopez-Chavez, supra, at 1043 (explaining that it is not only necessary to demonstrate that counsel’s ineffective assistance rendered proceedings unfair, but also that such deficient performance resulted in prejudice). She did not provide any evidence to demonstrate that she was eligible for and had a reasonable likelihood of receiving the waiver. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, supra, at 827 (explaining that it is necessary to establish both eligibility for relief and a plausibility that relief could have been granted to substantiate a finding of prejudice).
 
The respondent represented that she would file the waiver application and associated receipt with the Board with a motion to remand (Respondent’s Brief at 16). In our prior decision, we noted that we had not received any supplemental filings and to date we have not received evidence that such a waiver application was filed (BIA Dec. dated Oct. 10, 2014, at 3 n.1). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1130673 (BIA)

CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(A))
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 22, 2016. On appeal the respondent contends that he received ineffective assistance of counsel because his former counsel conceded that his June 2, 2015, conviction for Indecency with a Child in violation of Texas Penal Code § 21.11(a)(1) qualifies as a sexual abuse of a minor aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(A). See generally Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000). However, the respondent’s conviction qualifies as an aggravated felony. See United States v. Najera-Najera, 519 F.3d 509, 511 (5th Cir. 2008); see also United States v. Zavala-Sustaita, 214 F.3d 601 (5th Cir. 2000); Matter of Rodriguez-Rodriguez, 22 I&N Dec. 991 (BIA 1999). Thus, he did not receive ineffective assistance. The following order will be entered.

2017 WL 1130690 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HERIBERTO JACQUEZ VILLAREAL
File: AXXX XX3 104 - Dallas, TX
January 27, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vinesh Patel, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
On September 30, 2015, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s oral motion for continuance, and found the respondent removable as charged. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, now appeals. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings regarding witness credibility and what is likely to happen to the respondent, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015). We review all other issues, including questions of law, discretion, and judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a motion for continuance for good cause shown. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987). An Immigration Judge also has broad discretionary authority in determining whether an alien has demonstrated good cause. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983).
 
On November 8, 2012, the respondent pled guilty to theft under TEX. PENAL CODE § 31.03(e)(4)(A). The respondent sought a continuance to provide an opportunity for his attorney in his criminal case to file a motion for a new trial based on a claim that his prior criminal attorney was ineffective. The respondent claimed that the prior attorney was ineffective because he did not warn the respondent of the immigration consequences of his guilty plea (Respondent’s Br. at unnumbered 2; Tr. at 8).
 
The respondent’s desire to seek post-conviction relief does not amount to “““good cause” for a continuance. The fact that the respondent may be pursuing post-conviction relief in the form of a collateral attack on a conviction in state criminal court does not affect its finality for federal immigration purposes. See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506 (BIA 1992). The respondent’s speculation that his conviction might be invalid, including for reasons related to ineffective assistance of counsel as in Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010), does not change the finality for immigration purposes of such conviction, unless and until it were to be overturned by a criminal court (I.J. at 2; Respondent’s Br. at unnumbered 2). See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1997, 1996); see also Cabral v. Holder, 632 F.3d 886, 890 (5th Cir. 2011); Milton v. Holder, 425 F. App’x. 407, 409 (5th Cir. 2011) (stating that “Padilla also does not suggest that immigration proceedings must be stayed while a Padilla-type claim is being pursued in post[-]conviction proceedings.”). Moreover, the respondent acknowledged at the September 30, 2015, hearing that the attorney for his criminal case had not yet filed the motion for a new trial, and he has not provided any evidence of such filing on appeal (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 8, 12).
 
*2 The respondent argues that his case is distinguished from Cabral v. Holder, supra. In that case, the alien sought to hold his appeal before the Board in abeyance so that he could pursue a motion to vacate state convictions upon which his removability was based. The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, under whose jurisdiction this case arises, held that the Board properly denied the respondent’s request because it was speculative to conclude that the state court will vacate a state court conviction for reasons that remove it for immigration purposes. The respondent argues that his case is distinguishable because unlike in his case the alien in Cabral v. Holder, supra, did not collaterally attack his state conviction based on ineffective assistance of counsel, and the court did not analyze the request under Padilla v. Kentucky, supra (Respondent’s Br. at unnumbered 2). This, however, is a distinction without a difference because the action underlying both the respondent’s motion for a continuance and Cabral’s request to hold the appeal in abeyance was a collateral attack on the alien’s state court conviction.
 
For these reasons we conclude that the respondent did not demonstrate good cause for a continuance. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s ruling in this case.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1130690 (BIA)
End of Document
