2016 WL 8188647 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LAKHWINDER SINGH
File: AXXX XX1 929 - Los Angeles, CA
October 31, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert B. Jobe, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen substantially complies with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), in setting out a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Given the totality of circumstances and evidence presented, including the Department of Homeland Security’s lack of response or opposition to the pending motion, and the evidence reflecting that former counsel(s)’ representation may have affected the outcome of the respondent’s proceedings, we will reopen the proceedings under the provisions of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings on the respondent’s applications for relief. See also Correa-Rivera v. Holder, 706 F.3d 1128, 1133-34 (9th Cir. 2013) (explaining the prejudice results “when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.”) (citations omitted). In so remanding, we express no opinion on the ultimate outcome of these proceedings.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings not inconsistent with this order and the entry of a new decision.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 8188647 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFREDO TREJO BALLEZA
File: AXXX XX8 675 - Charlotte, NC
October 24, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mark J. Deyine, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Lecker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 This matter is before us on certification from the Immigration Judge, upon issuance of his decision of January 5, 2016. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel and was prejudiced by his former attorney, who did not present his cancellation of removal application nor his request for prosecutorial discretion (Respondent’s Brief at 11-21). The respondent requests a remand to present his application for cancellation of removal and request for prosecutorial discretion (Respondent’s Brief at 22).
 
The record shows that the respondent has supported his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with the appropriate documentation required under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). However, the respondent must also demonstrate that he was substantially prejudiced by the ineffective assistance. See Figeroa v. INS, 886 F.2d 76, 78 (4th Cir. 1989) (stating that to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must show not only ineffective representation, but also prejudice to him or her which occurred as a result of that ineffectiveness).
 
The respondent has not demonstrated that he is prima facie eligible for cancellation of removal (I.J. at 1). To date, the respondent has not submitted his application for cancellation of removal or sufficient supporting documentation to establish his eligibility for said relief. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (assertions of counsel are not evidence). Specifically, as the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent has not submitted evidence of his qualifying relatives nor provided proof of his continuous physical presence in the United States since 2004 (I.J. at 1). Thus, the respondent has not established that he is eligible for cancellation of removal.1
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, we do not find that the ineffective assistant of counsel described by the respondent established prejudice such that the outcome of his proceedings would have been different but for the ineffective assistance. Therefore, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Any request for a favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion would have to be addressed to the DHS.

2016 WL 7032762 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YONG TEAK LEE A.K.A. LEE YONG
File: AXXX XX6 569 - Atlanta, GA
October 24, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hiba Ghalib, Esquire
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of South Korea, filed a motion to reopen on August 10, 2016. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the filing. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent was charged with being subject to removal under section 237(a)(1)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i), in that he failed to comply with the conditions of nonimmigrant status. On November 5, 2015, the Immigration Judge granted a period of pre-conclusion voluntary departure until March 4, 2016, under section 240B(a) of the Act. It was noted that the respondent had waived the right to appeal to the Board. Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000) (grant of voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Act requires a waiver of the right to appeal); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(D).
 
Rather than depart as ordered, the respondent on March 4, 2016, filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. This was based on the fact that on February 17, 2016, the respondent married a United States citizen, and she had filed an immediate relative visa petition on his behalf with the DHS, United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). In a decision mailed on March 25, 2016, the Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), and because the respondent did not present evidence indicating a strong likelihood that his recent marriage is bona fide. The Immigration Judge also found that the respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and showed no prejudice from former counsel’s actions. Finally, the Immigration Judge denied the motion as a matter of discretion. We affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying reopening on July 12, 2016.
 
The respondent’s pending motion to reopen is untimely and barred by the numerical limitations on motions. Section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2)(subject to certain exceptions, an alien in removal proceedings may file only one motion to reopen removal proceedings, whether before the Board or Immigration Judge).
 
The pending time and number-barred motion to reopen also does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen these proceedings under our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). The August 10, 2016, motion speculates that approval of the immediate relative visa petition filed by the respondent’s wife is imminent. A check of the USCIS case status system using the receipt number provided by the respondent with his motion shows that, instead, the visa petition is still pending before USCIS. In any event, even if approved, becoming potentially eligible for adjustment of status is not uncommon and generally not a basis for granting an untimely motion to reopen. See generally Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009).
 
*2 The pending motion will, therefore, be denied.
 
ORDER: The number-barred motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 7032762 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BERNARD AKWASI ACHEAMPONG A.K.A. BENARD ACHEAMPONG A.K.A. BERNARD O. ACHEAMPONG
File: AXXX XX1 747 - Baltimore, MD
October 20, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Polina Tomashevsky, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(F))
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Ghana, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 19, 2015, denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider.1 The record does not contain a response from the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge improperly weighed the evidence in the record and, as such, erred in finding him ineligible for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i) (I.J.2 at 3-4, 6-8; Respondent’s Brief at 4-9). See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (stating that a motion to reconsider must include an allegation of material factual or legal error in the prior decision).2 Namely, the respondent argues that he was not convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude that bars his eligibility for this relief (Respondent’s Brief at 4-9). See section 245(a) of the Act (stating that, to be eligible for adjustment of status, an alien must be admissible to the United States for permanent residence); section 212(a)(2)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A) of the Act (stating that an alien who has been convicted an offense that constitutes a crime involving moral turpitude is inadmissible).
 
The respondent pled guilty to theft on April 15, 2002, and was sentenced to probation before judgment (“PBJ”) (I.J.1 at 3-4). He does not contest the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that this offense constitutes a crime involving moral turpitude (I.J.2 at 6-7). However, he contends that, on November 14, 2006, his PBJ disposition was entered nolle prosequi based on a defect in the underlying criminal proceedings (I.J.1 at 2). As such, he argues that his judgment does not constitute a conviction for immigration purposes (I.J.1 at 2). See section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A) (defining a “conviction” under the Act); Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621, 624 (BIA 2003) (stating that when a court vacates a conviction based on a defect in the underlying criminal proceedings, the alien no longer has a “conviction” within the meaning of the Act).
 
*2 The Immigration Judge properly weighed the evidence in the record in determining that the respondent was convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude (I.J.2 at 6-8). From the outset, we agree that the respondent bears the burden of demonstrating his eligibility for this relief (I.J.2 at 6; Respondent’s Brief at 4-6). See Salem v. Holder, 647 F.3d 111, 115 (4th Cir. 2011) (stating that the alien bears the burden of demonstrating that a mandatory bar to eligibility for relief does not apply).
 
To meet his burden, the respondent submitted a letter from a Maryland criminal attorney, stating that a PBJ disposition is only entered nolle prosequi when a case is subject to a post-conviction action that corrects a constitutional violation (I.J.2 at 6-7). The respondent also submitted a letter from his former attorney, stating that this attorney “argued and got [the respondent’s] theft conviction quashed” pursuant to a coram nobis petition (I.J.2 at 7). Finally, the respondent submitted excerpts from the Maryland Court Rules and Legislative Handbook, which provide a description of avenues for post-conviction relief (I.J.2 at 7).
 
The evidence submitted is not sufficient to meet the respondent’s burden (I.J.2 at 7). The respondent has provided no evidence or testimony establishing the grounds on which his specific PBJ disposition was entered nolle prosequi (I.J.2 at 6-7). The letter from the criminal attorney is based on the attorney’s personal experience and does not discuss the respondent’s matter (I.J.2 at 6-7). Similarly, the letter from the respondent’s former attorney does not describe the details of the case or the grounds on which he quashed the respondent’s conviction (I.J.2 at 7).3 Accordingly, these letters in combination with the excerpts from the Maryland Court Rules and Legislative Handbook do not establish that the respondent’s PBJ disposition was entered nolle prosequi based on a defect in his underlying criminal proceedings (I.J. at 7). See Matter of Pickering, supra, at 624.
 
The respondent thus has not established that the Immigration Judge erred in weighing the evidence in the record and concluding that he was convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude that barred his eligibility for adjustment of status (I.J.2 at 6-8). See Matter of O-S-G-, supra. Based on the foregoing, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider and ordering him removed from the United States (I.J.2 at 9).
 
The respondent also asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for a continuance to allow United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) to reconsider its revocation of his Petition for Alien Relative (Form I-130) (Respondent’s Brief at 9-10). Nevertheless, the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s denial. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983) (stating that an alien must establish that the denial of a continuance caused actual prejudice and materially affected the outcome of the case). As discussed above, the respondent has not demonstrated that he is eligible for adjustment of status, as he was convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude that rendered him inadmissible. Therefore, the USCIS’s forthcoming decision regarding the respondent’s Form I-130 will not impact the outcome of his case. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

“I.J.1” refers to the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the respondent was convicted of a theft offense for immigration purposes, dated March 19, 2015. “I.J.2” refers to the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider, dated August 19, 2015.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOHN ADRIAN MALDONADO-GUINANZACA
File: AXXX XX3 454 - New Orleans, LA
October 18, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pablo Fernandez-Herrera, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charlotte Marquez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated December 5, 2014, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen to rescind the in absentia removal order that was entered on June 2, 2014. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the respondent’s appeal. The record will be remanded.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We are unable to meaningfully review the December 5, 2014, decision of the Immigration Judge as that order consists of a two-sentence conclusion of law without any findings of fact or analysis (Resp. Br. at 7-8). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (d)(3)(iv); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 463-65 (BIA 2002) (Immigration Judges must include in their decisions clear and complete findings of fact that are supported by the record and comply with controlling law). The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had not met his burden to establish ineffective assistance of his former counsel in order to toll the filing deadline for a motion to reopen. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly, J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
However, the Immigration Judge did not render any findings regarding what requirements of Matter Lozada, supra, for an ineffective assistance claim, have or have not been met.1 In this regard, there is evidence of record that the respondent submitted an affidavit setting forth his agreement with his former counsel, that the respondent has informed his former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance through a complaint with a state bar, and the respondent has submitted a formal complaint against his former counsel with the appropriate disciplinary authority (Resp. Br. at 12; DHS Br. at 2-4).2 Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Moreover, the Immigration Judge should determine whether the respondent has met his burden to establish that his former counsel’s ineffective assistance resulted in actual prejudice (Resp. Br. at 12-13). Id. at 640. Additionally, if the Immigration Judge finds that the ineffective assistance of counsel claim has been established, he should then determine if equitable tolling applies. Therefore, the record must be remanded for the preparation of a decision by the Immigration Judge that will permit meaningful appellate review. By remanding the record, the Board does not express any opinion on the final outcome of this matter.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD

Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647 (5th Cir. 2014) (finding an alien’s argument that strict compliance with the Lozada requirements is not necessary was without merit and distinguished the Fifth Circuit’s precedent decisions from those of the Ninth Circuit that adopted a different approach in applying the Lozada requirements, where the Ninth Circuit hears ineffective assistance of counsel claims even when a petitioner has arguably failed to comply with the Lozada requirements).

2016 WL 7032796 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALMA SUSANA CORTEZ MENDOZA
File: AXXX XX9 975 - Phoenix, AZ
October 14, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ray A. Ybarra Maldonado, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Motion to administratively close proceedings
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the April 24, 2015, decision of the Immigration Judge denying her motion to administratively close proceedings.1 The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a response. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s discretionary decision to deny the respondent’s request for administrative closure. See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688, 696 (BIA 2012) (outlining factors for evaluating whether administrative closure is warranted, including the likelihood of success on any underlying requests for relief).2 We observe that administrative closure is used to temporarily remove a case from an Immigration Judge’s active calendar or from the Board’s docket. See Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 480 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Avetisyan, supra, at 692. The administrative closure of a case does not result in a final order. It is merely an administrative convenience that allows cases to be removed from the calendar in appropriate situations, subject to being recalendared at a later date. See Matter of Amico, 19 I&N Dec. 652, 654 n.1 (BIA 1988).
 
In the present case, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to administratively close proceedings because she had no apparent relief available to her other than voluntary departure. The DHS opposed administrative closure in this case, and the DHS Office of the Chief Counsel had declined to exercise prosecutorial discretion (I.J. at 6; Tr. at 46). While the respondent argues that she is eligible for prosecutorial discretion, the DHS has sole authority to exercise prosecutorial discretion in this matter (Respondent’s Br. at 12-13). Neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over issues of prosecutorial discretion. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348, 349-50 (BIA 1982).
 
The respondent also contends that her case should be administratively closed in light of pending litigation in Puente Arizona v. Arpaio, 76 F.Supp.3d 833 (D. Ariz. 2015), or in the alternative, be held in abeyance. On January 5, 2015, the District Court for Arizona found that Arizona’s identity theft laws were facially preempted by federal immigration policy and granted the plaintiffs--an immigrant advocacy organization, individual “unauthorized aliens,” and taxpayers of Maricopa County--a preliminary injunction preventing the Arizona government defendants from enforcing the challenged provisions. Id. at 869. The respondent argues that a favorable decision in this case could result in the vacation of her conviction, where she was convicted of criminal impersonation in Arizona (Respondent’s Br. at 7; Exhs. 4-5). After the respondent submitted her brief on August 26, 2015, however, the Ninth Circuit reversed the district court’s holding that the laws are likely facially preempted and vacated the preliminary injunction. Puente Arizona v. Arpaio, 821 F.3d 1098 (9th Cir. 2016). As such, the respondent’s argument on this issue is moot.
 
*2 Finally, to the extent the respondent asserts that her due process rights were violated in the proceedings below, we are unpersuaded. See Cruz Rendon v. Holder, 603 F.3d 1104 (9th Cir. 2010) (holding that in order to prevail on a due process claim that she was denied a full and fair removal hearing, an alien must demonstrate that the challenged proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that she was prevented from reasonably presenting her case; and that she suffered prejudice, such that the outcome of the proceeding may have been affected by the alleged violation). The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge acted in a prosecutorial manner rather than remaining neutral, and that she was questioned by the Immigration Judge, without the presence of counsel, despite appearing in court under the belief that she would be represented (Respondent’s Br. at 4-5, 11).
 
We do not find a basis to conclude that the Immigration Judge’s management of the record establishes a due process violation. We have reviewed the record and find no basis to conclude that the Immigration Judge’s questioning or comments in any way exceeded judicial bounds. An Immigration Judge has the authority to “““interrogate, examine, and cross-examine the alien” in removal proceedings. See section 240(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(1); see generally Matter of Rhee, 16 I&N Dec. 607 (BIA 1978).
 
Furthermore, to the extent that the respondent made statements without the presence of counsel, such as admitting to her convictions, the record supports those statements, and the respondent has not argued otherwise (Tr. at 19-21 (Mar. 8, 2013); Exhs. 4-5). Insofar as the respondent claims that the counsel she hired did not properly represent her, the record does not reflect that she was in fact represented at the time of her final hearing; instead, it reflects that the court granted a motion to withdraw from her former counsel, but no Notice of Appearance on her behalf was filed subsequently by another representative until her appeal to the Board (Exhs. 8-9). The respondent has also not complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, or demonstrated that she suffered resultant prejudice.3 Overall, we conclude that remanded proceedings are not warranted on this basis.
 
Based on the foregoing, the discretionary benefit of administrative closure was not appropriate in this case, and the motion to administrative close was properly denied. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, supra, at 505 (once the DHS institutes proceedings, an Immigration Judge must order an alien deported if the evidence supports the charge); Matter of Avetisyan, supra, at 696. The respondent provided evidence that she paid the required voluntary departure bond; therefore, we will reinstate the period of voluntary departure. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. See section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if she provides to the DHS such evidence of her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*4 Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

At the final hearing on April 24, 2015, the Immigration Judge orally denied the respondent’s motion for administrative closure. In an oral decision and form order issued the same day, the Immigration Judge also granted post-conclusion voluntary departure to the respondent.

2

In Matter of Avetisyan, the Board held that, in determining whether administrative closure of proceedings is appropriate, an Immigration Judge or the Board should weigh all the relevant factors presented in the case. These factors include, but are not limited to: (1) the reason administrative closure is sought; (2) the basis for any opposition to administrative closure; (3) the likelihood the respondent will succeed on any petition, application, or other action he or she is pursuing outside of removal proceedings; (4) the anticipated duration of the closure; (5) the responsibility of either party, if any, in contributing to any current or anticipated delay; and (6) the ultimate outcome of removal proceedings (for example, termination of the proceedings or entry of a removal order) when the case is recalendared before the Immigration Judge or the appeal is reinstated before the Board. Id. at 695.

3

Specifically, the respondent has not presented an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with her prior counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations the prior counsel did or did not make, the respondent does not indicate that her prior counsel was informed of the allegations leveled against him or her and has been given an opportunity to respond, and the respondent does not indicate whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Additionally, because the respondent has not shown that, but for her prior counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, she would have obtained post-conviction relief, she has not demonstrated that she was prejudiced by her prior counsel’s ineffective assistance.

2016 WL 7032798 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADELIYA MUBARIKA AHMAD
File: AXXX XX7 241 - Atlanta, GA
October 14, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kenneth S. Levine, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 5, 2015, denying her motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia for her failure to appear at the hearing on June 18, 2009. We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
In her motion to reopen, the respondent asserts that she failed to appear at her removal hearing due to exceptional circumstances, specifically, the ineffective assistance of her former attorney, and because she lacked notice. In denying her motion, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent received sufficient notice and that she failed to use due diligence in filing her motion to reopen. However, the Immigration Judge did not address the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel or her request for sua sponte reopening. Under the particular circumstances of this case, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of this claim and the entry of a new decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (limiting the Board’s fact-finding ability on appeal).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 7032798 (BIA)
End of Document

2016 WL 7032807 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SARA LISSETTE PENA-JIMENEZ
File: AXXX XX6 496 - Los Angeles, CA
October 13, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul Clemente Supple, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on July 5, 2007, after not appearing at her hearing. She filed a motion to reopen on August 6, 2015, and the respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 29, 2015, denying the motion. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that she did not receive the Notice of Hearing because it was mailed to her former address (Resp. Brief at 11). She further argues that her former attorney committed ineffective assistance of counsel by not raising the issue of lack of notice in her motion to reopen (Resp. Brief at 11-12). However, the respondent has not complied with any of the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. See Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893 (9th Cir. 2008) (setting forth the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, the respondent has not submitted a sworn statement attesting to lack of notice, or explaining why she did not notify the Immigration Court of her change of address from that listed on the Notice to Appear. We further note that the Notice of Hearing for July 5, 2007, was not returned as undeliverable. The statements of counsel in her appellate brief are not evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Consequently, we decline to reopen or remand these proceedings based on the lack of notice.
 
In addition, the respondent argues on appeal that she established an exceptional situation to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s and the Immigration Judge’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). She states that she was not notified of her hearing and is eligible for benefits under the Deferred Action for Parents of Americans and Lawful Permanent Residents (DAPA) program. The respondent further states that she is the parent of three United States citizen children who were born subsequent to the hearing and one of whom is being treated for cancer. The respondent argues that these proceedings should be reopened based on humanitarian considerations.
 
*2 We are sympathetic to the respondent and especially to her child who is battling a serious disease. However, the respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear on March 2, 2006, waited more than 9 years to file her motion to reopen, and she did not provide an explanation for her lack of due diligence. In addition, the Immigration Judge has no jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s eligibility for DAPA; the application for such a discretionary status or any similar exercise of prosecutorial discretion should be directed to the Department of Homeland Security. In light of the foregoing, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny sua sponte reopening of these proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 7032807 (BIA)
End of Document

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PABLO AGUILAR-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX0 019 - San Diego, CA
October 11, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James Bradley Rudolph, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Amelia C. Anderson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 29, 2014, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and remand and request for stay of removal. The record will be remanded.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
In considering this appeal, we note our decision in Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994). Therein, we indicated that when a motion to reopen deportation proceedings is denied, the Immigration Judge must identify and fully explain the reasons for such decision; otherwise, the parties are deprived of a fair opportunity to contest the Immigration Judge’s determination on appeal, and the Board is unable to meaningfully exercise its responsibility of reviewing the decision in light of the arguments advanced on appeal. See also Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468 (BIA 1999).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen on a form order, checking the box that states: “[t]he court agrees with the reasons stated in the opposition to the motion,” adding the handwritten notation “independently and cumulatively.” The Immigration Judge also checked the box that states: “[t]he motion is untimely per,” adding the handwritten notation “8 C.F.R. 1003.23(b)(1) and tolling is not appropriate for the reasons stated in the DHS opposition.” The Immigration Judge failed to specifically address the issues raised in the respondent’s motion, including the allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel regarding prior counsel’s conduct, and his claim that he was prejudiced by such conduct. We find that the Immigration Judge’s decision provides an insufficient basis upon which the Board can adequately conduct a meaningful review.
 
Accordingly, we find it necessary to remand the record for inclusion of an appropriate decision by the Immigration Judge. The respondent and the Department of Homeland Security should be served with a copy of the decision and should thereafter be given an opportunity to submit briefs in accordance with the regulations. The record should then be forwarded to the Board for further consideration.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.
 
Blair O’Connor
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 6519912 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: THOMAS ROCHA GONZALEZ A.K.A. THOMAS ROCHA GONZALEZ
File: AXXX XX2 784 - Huntsville, TX
October 6, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carrie Law
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on July 18, 2002, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s application for waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) and 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), (h), and ordering him removed. On May 26, 2016, more than 13½ years after the Board’s order, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the motion, the respondent argues that his proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance by attorney John Perez, who represented him before the Immigration Judge, and attorney Yalia Guerrero, who represented him on appeal. The respondent argues that the former counsels provided ineffective assistance because they did not argue that the respondent’s 2001 conviction was not an aggravated felony or a removable offense and they did not advise the respondent to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), or to terminate the proceedings
 
However, the respondent’s motion was filed more than 13/4 years after the Board’s final administrative decision, and the respondent has not addressed the untimeliness of his motion. In Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, __ F.3d __, 2016 WL 4056051 (5th Cir. July 28, 2016), the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit held that a litigant is entitled to equitable tolling of limitations only if the litigant establishes two elements: (1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing. Whether equitable tolling is appropriate in a case depends on individual facts and circumstances of the case. Although the respondent argues that his former counsels provided ineffective assistance by failing to make certain arguments, the respondent has not explained how he has acted with due diligence in pursuing his ineffective assistance of counsel claims during the period of more than 13½ years since the Board’s prior decision.
 
*2 Furthermore, we are not persuaded that the respondent has shown that his former counsels provided ineffective assistance at the time of his hearing and appeal. The respondent’s arguments regarding ineffective assistance of counsel are based on recent development in caselaw regarding the definition of “““crime of violence” aggravated felony and divisibility of criminal statutes. Descamps v. U.S., 133 S.Ct. 2276 (2013); United States v. Gonzalez-Longoria, 813 F.3d 225 (5th Cir. 2016), on reh’g en banc, No. 15-40041, 2016 WL 4169127 (5th Cir. Aug. 5, 2016); Gomez-Perez v. Lynch, __ F.3d __, No. 14-60808, 2016 WL 3709757 (5th Cir. July 11, 2016); see also Dimaya v. Lynch, 803 F.3d 1110 (9th Cir. 2015), petition for cert. pending (No. 15-1498, Jun. 10, 2016); United States v. Vivas-Ceja, 808 F.3d 719, 720 (7th Cir. 2015). These cases had not yet been published in 2002 but were only published many years after the respondent’s proceedings were concluded. The respondent has not shown how a reasonably competent attorney should have been expected to present arguments based on these cases in 2002, or how his former counsels’ representation rose to the level of ineffective assistance for not making these arguments in 2002. The respondent argues that attorney Perez was not even clear whether the respondent was seeking a waiver under section 212(c) or 212(h) of the Act. However, the Immigration Judge considered both waivers under the legal standard applicable at the time (I.J. at 2).
 
Based on the above, the respondent has not shown that his proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance by his former counsels. The respondent also has not shown that his case presents an exceptional situation that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the power to reopen sua sponte “is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied as untimely. The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

Seth L. Reszko, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision granting him post-conclusion voluntary departure. This proceeding was previously before the Board when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal as to the merits of his adjustment of status application, but remanded the record for reconsideration of his application for voluntary departure at the conclusion of proceedings under section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). The appeal is dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the Immigration Judge’s credibility determination, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). The respondent has the burden of proving his eligibility for the relief sought. Sections 208(b)(1)(B), 241(b)(3)(C) of the Act; Matter of J-B-N & S-M-, 24 I& N. Dec. 208 (BIA 2007).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent contends that, on remand, the Immigration Judge erred in declining to consider his request for pre-conclusion voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Act. However, the respondent waived this issue. The respondent was represented by counsel during his hearing on the merits, and his attorney only requested voluntary departure as an alternative form of relief at the end of the hearing (Tr. dated 10/6/14 at 6). Further, in his first appeal, the respondent did not argue that he was eligible for pre-conclusion voluntary departure. As such, the respondent has waived this issue. Matter of L-G-H-, 26 I&N Dec. 365, 366 n.1 (BIA 2014); Matter of R-A-M-, 25 I&N Dec. 657, 658 n.8 (BIA 2012).1
 
Moreover, the respondent did not demonstrate that he was eligible for pre-conclusion voluntary departure. He did not request such relief at his initial merits hearing; he made additional requests for relief, and did not withdraw such requests prior to the Immigration Judge’s original denial of relief; and he did not waive appeal of all issues (Tr. dated 6/2/14 and 10/6/14 at 1-20). Section 240B(b) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(A), (B), and (D). Accordingly, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s grant of post conclusion voluntary departure.
 
*2 The respondent also contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying a continuance because he was not eligible for adjustment of status due to a false claim to United States citizenship. We previously addressed these issues (BIA Dec. dated 1/19/16 at 2-3), and we will not revisit them.
 
The respondent’s brief erroneously requests in its conclusion certain relief that the respondent did not seek (Respondent’s Brief at 7). As such, there is no need to address this request for relief. Since the Immigration Judge granted the respondent post-conclusion voluntary departure, and the respondent attached evidence that he paid the requisite bond, we will reinstate voluntary departure on appeal. Accordingly, the following order will be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*3 Roger Pauley

His attorney does not allege that he provided ineffective assistance of counsel. Moreover, under the circumstances of this case, we find no merit to the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge had an independent obligation to pursue pre-conclusion voluntary departure on the respondent’s behalf.

IN RE: RIGOBERTO ANGEL-NUNEZ

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sylvia L. Esparza, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 On June 8, 2015, the Board upheld an Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. The respondent had sought reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
This matter is now before the Board pursuant to a July 27, 2016, order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which granted a motion to remand filed by the Government. The Government sought a remand “[b]ecause it appears that the Board may have used a more stringent standard than required by Ninth Circuit law when adjudicating [the respondent’s] motion to reopen[.]” The Government also noted that the Board may not make an adverse credibility finding on a motion to reopen but must accept evidence, and an alien’s statements, as true unless inherently unbelievable.
 
The respondent is ultimately seeking reopening, in part, to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on a fear of persecution on account of his membership in a particular social group. Since the Immigration Judge’s original decision in this matter, the Ninth Circuit has issued intervening precedent regarding particular social group based asylum and withholding of removal claims. See Pirir-Boc v. Holder, 750 F.3d 1077 (9th Cir. 2014). The resolution of the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim will require consideration of such intervening precedent. Additionally, further fact finding may be required.
 
Given the totality of the circumstances, we deem it appropriate to remand this matter to the Immigration Judge to readjudicate the respondent’s motion to reopen, in a manner that is consistent with the Ninth Circuit’s order granting the Government’s motion to remand, as well as current Ninth Circuit precedent on particular social group claims. Upon remand, both parties shall not be precluded from proffering additional evidence, both testimonial and documentary.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
John Guendelsberger

2016 WL 6519948 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN SANDOVAL-CHAVEZ
File: AXXX XX9 315 - El Paso, TX
October 3, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Felipe D.J. Millan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rachel McDonald
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on March 28, 2016, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and reinstated the 60-day period of voluntary departure granted by the Immigration Judge. On July 20, 2016, almost 4 months after the Board’s order, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent argues that an alien “may be entitled to reopen the case out of time” if the alien is deprived of his due process rights due to ineffective assistance of counsel and suffers prejudice (Motion, at 5-6). However, the respondent cites no caselaw from the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit in support of this proposition. The respondent offers no explanation on whether or why he could not file a timely motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. To the extent we construe the respondent’s argument as urging us to apply equitable tolling of the motion to reopen time limitations, the respondent has not explained the legal or factual basis of any equitable tolling in his case. The respondent also argues that time and number limitations shall not apply to a motion to reopen agreed upon by all parties and jointly filed (Motion, at 6). However, the DHS has not joined in the current motion but has opposed the motion as noted above. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is untimely, and will be denied as untimely.
 
Even if the respondent’s motion were timely filed, the respondent has not shown that his proceedings should be reopened based on the ineffective assistance by attorney Enrique Garcia, his former counsel. The respondent argues that the former counsel provided ineffective assistance because he: (1) did not pursue his derivative citizenship claim through his father, who naturalized in 2001; (2) did not contest the false claim of citizenship charge; (3) did not pursue his adjustment of status claim based on the visa petition filed by his United States citizen spouse; (4) did not pursue benefits under the Deferred Action for Childhood Arrivals (“DACA”); and (5) did not move to terminate the proceedings. We will address these claims in turn.
 
First, as to the derivative citizenship claim, contrary to the respondent’s assertions, the former counsel did make the derivative citizenship claim and submitted documents in support of this claim, as noted in the Board’s prior decision. The former counsel also stated that the respondent had previously submitted an Application for Certificate of Citizenship (Form N-600) with the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) shortly after his father’s naturalization, and that he was again in the process of re-applying for the certificate and to determine what happened to the initial request (Resp. App. Brief, “Argument”). Therefore, the record does not support the respondent’s assertion that the former counsel failed to file an Application for Certificate of Citizenship or pursue the derivative citizenship claim (Motion, at 9). In any event, as noted in the Board’s prior decision (fn. 3), the respondent may file a motion to reopen and to terminate, if and when he is issued a Certificate of Citizenship by the USCIS. Therefore, the respondent did not show that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s failure to pursue the derivative citizenship claim.
 
*2 Next, as to the false claim of United States citizenship issue, the respondent does not deny that in May 2005 he made a false claim of United States citizenship before immigration officers.1 However, the respondent argues that the former counsel should have argued that the respondent’s false claim falls within the exception set forth in section 212(a)(6)(C)(ii)(II) of the Act because the respondent’s father was naturalized in 2001 (Motion, at 10-11). Nevertheless, the respondent has not shown that, if the former counsel had argued that the respondent falls within this exception, such argument may have been upheld. Section 212(a)(6)(C)(ii)(II) of the Act requires, inter alia, that “each natural parent of the alien (emphasis added)” be a United States citizen, either by birth or naturalization. Although the record shows that the respondent’s father was a naturalized United States citizen when the respondent made the claim of citizenship, the record does not show, and the respondent has not argued, that his mother was also a United States citizen at that time. Therefore, the respondent has not shown that he falls within this exception, or that his argument would have been successful if he had made an argument that he falls within this exception. In addition, since the respondent is inadmissible based on the false claim of United States citizen citizenship, his application for adjustment of status would have been denied even if he had applied for this form of relief.
 
As to the respondent’s argument that the former counsel failed to apply for DACA, even assuming that the respondent can establish his eligibility for DACA, this is a matter within the authority of the USCIS, not the Board or the Immigration Judge. Furthermore, the respondent is not prevented from seeking DACA by the fact that he is under an order of removal.
 
The respondent next argues that the former counsel should have moved for termination of these proceedings, but we are not persuaded that a motion to terminate may have been granted based on the record of this case. As noted in the Board’s prior decision and again in this decision, if and when the respondent is issued a Certificate of Citizenship by the USCIS, he may file a motion to reopen and terminate. Based on the above, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by any ineffective assistance of his former counsel. See Ogbemudia v. INS, 988 F.2d 595 (5th Cir. 1993).
 
The respondent also urges in his motion for stay of removal that he should be granted a stay of removal so that he can have the opportunity to file an application for cancellation of removal (Motion for Stay, at 5). The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely as noted above, and this aspect of the motion does not fall within any exception to the motion to reopen time limitations enumerated in section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). Furthermore, the respondent has not shown prima facie eligibility for cancellation of removal, nor did he submit an application and supporting documents for this form of relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c)(1) (requiring that a motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of applying for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation).
 
*3 Finally, we do not find that the respondent’s case presents an exceptional situation that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied. The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The DHS argues that the respondent was never charged for false claim of United States citizenship thus he could not have contested the charge (DHS Opp. at 5). However, the former counsel conceded that the respondent was inadmissible based on the false claim of United States citizen citizenship and he is not eligible for adjustment of status, therefore he will not seek adjustment of status (Tr. at 11). Section 212(a)(6)(C)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(ii). We therefore construe the respondent’s argument as an argument that the former counsel should have argued that the respondent is not inadmissible based on the false claim of citizenship.

2016 WL 6519970 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DARYS REY-LOZANO A.K.A. DARYS REY-LORENZO A.K.A. DARYS REY GARCIA
File: AXXX XX1 714 - New York, NY
September 27, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Davar Yusufi, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of August 4, 2015. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s July 9, 2015, motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia on August 12, 2005. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a reply to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen alleges that her failure to appear for her August 12, 2005, hearing was due to exceptional circumstances.1 The respondent alleges ineffective assistance of counsel by a non-attorney who held himself out to be an attorney. Specifically, the respondent asserts that she hired Paul Falcon and they were to meet across the street from the Immigration Court on August 12, 2005, but he did not show up, and when she called him, he advised her not to go to her hearing or she would be arrested and deported. A claim of “ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an ‘exceptional circumstance’ warranting the reopening of a deportation order entered in absentia.” Arts v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595, 596 (2d Cir. 2008) (citation omitted).
 
A motion to reopen alleging exceptional circumstances for a failure to appear at a removal hearing must be filed within 180 days of the order of removal, and only one such motion is allowed. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i) & 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent’s motion claiming that she did not appear at her hearing because of ineffective assistance by Mr. Falcon, was untimely, as it was filed well beyond 180 days after the removal order was entered. However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case lies, has recognized that equitable tolling may apply in some circumstances. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 135 (2d Cir. 2000). The respondent is required to demonstrate “due diligence” in pursuing her claim during “both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed.” Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008); see also Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006) (requiring an alien to demonstrate due diligence independent from the requirement of demonstrating ineffective assistance of former counsel).
 
*2 The respondent here has not demonstrated or even argued that she exercised due diligence in pursuing her claim (I.J. at 2-3). The respondent stated in her affidavit supporting her motion to reopen that after failing to appear at her hearing, she went to the Fasano, Madeo law firm and informed them she had an in absentia order of deportation but they asked for too much money. The respondent has not indicated that she took any other measures to secure new counsel or otherwise pursue her claim herself. Instead, the respondent waited to seek reopening until approximately 10 years after entry of her removal order. Consequently, equitable tolling is not appropriate here. See Jin Bo Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154, 156-57 (2d Cir. 2006); Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008) (holding that petitioner failed to exercise due diligence when, after he should have known of his initial counsel’s ineffectiveness, he waited fourteen months to pursue the claim).
 
When an alien brings a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel stemming from the actions of a non-attorney immigration consultant, he must still make an effort to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See, e.g., Omar v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 647, 651 (2d Cir. 2008) (finding that the Board’s decision to deny the alien’s ineffective assistance claim against a non-attorney immigration consultant based on non-compliance with Lozada was “““neither arbitrary nor capricious”). Here, the respondent has failed to comply with the Lozada requirements insofar has she has not indicated that she informed Mr. Falcon of the allegations against him and she has further not indicated that she filed a complaint with the relevant state disciplinary authorities. The respondent has thus not even substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, supra. See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46-47 (2d Cir. 2005).
 
The respondent has proffered evidence on appeal. We do not consider evidence first submitted on appeal; rather, we review the record before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 73-74 & n.10 (BIA 1984); Matter of Maim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). Even if we were to construe the respondent’s additional evidence as a motion to remand, the respondent has not shown that this evidence is new or previously unavailable, or that it could possibly alter the decision in this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (holding that a party who seeks a remand to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case). Therefore, we find that a remand is not warranted.
 
*3 Lastly, we find that the approval of the respondent’s visa petition does not warrant reopening and remand under Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). That ease provides that an alien may reopen for purposes of pursuing previously unavailable relief in circumstances where he was not given oral warnings in his native language of the consequences of failing to appear at a deportation hearing and where the motion complies with the general time limits governing motions to reopen. Neither condition is satisfied here.
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e) (5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the record does not contain the notice of hearing for the August 12, 2005, hearing, we find no prejudice therefrom. The respondent does not allege lack of notice, and in fact asserts that she was aware of her August 12, 2005, hearing and appeared outside the Immigration Court on that date. See Garcia-Villeda v. Mukasey, 531 F.3d 141, 149 (2d Cir. 2008) (even if inadequacies in alien’s removal proceeding amounted to fundamental procedural errors, errors did not prejudice alien); United States v. Fernandez-Antonia, 278 F.3d 150, 159-60 (2d Cir. 2002) (an alien must show prejudice, which in turn requires a showing that but for the alleged constitutional violation, the result in his case might have been different).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IDRISSA SAWADOGO
File: AXXX XX0 351 - Detroit, MI
September 27, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Caridad Pastor Cardinale, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rosario Shoudy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
 
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 7, 2015, decision denying his second motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against him on March 1, 2007, after he failed to appear for his scheduled hearing. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the respondent was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on March 1, 2007, when he failed to appear for his scheduled hearing. The respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen and rescind the order on September 12, 2014, asking the Immigration Judge to exercise her sua sponte authority. On November 13, 2014, that motion was denied. The respondent then filed a motion to reconsider which was denied by the Immigration Judge on December 31, 2014. The respondent then filed the instant motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order, his second, on October 13, 2015.
 
Through the instant motion, the respondent claimed that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel because his prior attorney did not submit all of the documentation necessary with his first motion to reopen and failed to appeal the denial of his motion to reconsider (I.J. at 6). See Respondent’s Brief at 5-7. The respondent also, for the first time, claimed that he did not receive proper notice of his March 1, 2007, hearing. See id. at 4-5. According to the respondent, because the Notice of Hearing was returned to the Immigration Court as “undeliverable as addressed, no forwarding order on file,” the Immigration Court must have improperly addressed the notice and, thus, his non-receipt was “through no fault of his own.” See id. at 5.
 
Notwithstanding the respondent’s contentions on appeal, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his second motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order entered against him on March 1, 2007 (I.J. at 5-6). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2016) (de novo review). The respondent is not entitled to rescission of a removal order where the failure to receive actual notice of the time of the hearing is the result of his failure to comply with the obligation to keep the Immigration Court apprised of his current mailing address. The alien who evades notice cannot reopen the removal hearing. See Thompson v. Lynch, 788 F.3d 638 (6th Cir. 2015) (alien’s obligation under Immigration and Nationality Act to ensure that the government had an address at which he could be reached throughout the removal proceedings included the duty to correct the incorrect address listed on his notice to appear).
 
*2 We agree with the Immigration Judge that service of the Notice of Hearing was properly mailed to the last address provided by the respondent. Furthermore, as is the case here, if an alien does not fulfill his obligation to report his address change, notice of the hearing is not required pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(B), and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Service by mail is sufficient if the record contains proof of attempted delivery to the last address provided by the alien in accordance with section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(F). Section 239(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(c).
 
The respondent does not dispute that the Notice of Hearing was mailed to his address of record at the time it was issued, and the record contains proof of attempted delivery to the address provided by the respondent as reflected in the Form I-213 (Exh. 3). Therefore, the respondent can be charged with receiving proper notice, whether or not he actually received the Notice of Hearing. Sanchez v. Holder, 627 F.3d 226 (6th Cir. 2010) (initiating deportation proceedings in absentia does not depend on whether the alien actually receives notice of a deportation hearing, but rather on whether the government properly mails the notice according to the statute).
 
Regarding the respondent’s contention that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel as to his first motion to reopen and the appeal of his motion to reconsider, these allegations had no bearing on the respondent’s encounters with immigration officers when he was served with the Notice to Appear and was released on his own recognizance. On both of those occasions, the respondent was advised of his address obligations. Consequently, the Notice of Hearing was properly mailed to the respondent at the address he provided, and the respondent has not established that non-delivery was not due to his own fault (I.J. at 5-6). Therefore, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
