** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PORFIRIO NAHUN ESCOTO-SIERRA
File: AXXX XX9 874 - New Orleans, LA
September 22, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicole T. Bowyer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert Weir
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)] - Controlled substance violation
 
212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] (lodged) - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, appeals the Immigration Judge’s February 26, 2015, decision ordering him removed. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel.1 The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a motion for summary affirmance. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion will be denied.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In the Notice of Appeal, the respondent alleges that he did not have adequate time to prepare for his hearing because on February 9, 2015, he received the court’s February 4, 2015, notice of hearing, informing him that his master calendar hearing was rescheduled to February 26, 2015, approximately 1 year sooner than his previously scheduled February 24, 2016, hearing. Specifically, the respondent alleges that he was prejudiced by the change in hearing date because he did not have adequate time to marry his fiancée or to obtain post-conviction relief. Nonetheless, the respondent did not make these arguments or request a continuance before the Immigration Judge, and we will not consider these arguments now (Tr. at 9-17). See Matter of Jimenez, 21 I&N Dec. 567, 570 n.2 (BIA 1996) (providing that the Board will not consider arguments made for the first time on appeal).
 
In his motion to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent asserts that his former counsel erred in his representation when he sent a colleague to appear at the respondent’s master calendar hearing on February 26, 2015, without the respondent’s prior knowledge or consent (Resp. Br. at 2). He further asserts that his colleague was unfamiliar with immigration proceedings, was unaware of the type of relief for which the respondent was eligible, and because of these factors, the Immigration Judge ordered him removed (Resp. Br. at 3).
 
*2 In presenting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must comply with certain procedural requirements. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (stating that in order to prevail on a motion to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must establish and include the following: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not); Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647-48 (5th Cir. 2014) (making mandatory strict compliance with the Lozada requirements and emphasizing that former counsel must be informed of the allegations against them before the allegations are presented in a brief to the Board).
 
Here, we find that the respondent has not substantially met these procedural requirements. Although the respondent has submitted a copy of a complaint form detailing the allegations against his former counsel, he has not submitted an affidavit describing the facts and his agreement with his former counsel, nor has he provided any evidence that his former counsel has been made aware of the allegations against him and given an opportunity to respond, either after or before he filed his brief to the Board. See id; see also INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984) (statements by counsel in a brief are not evidence); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). The respondent has also not explained how he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638 (describing that in order to prevail on a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must also show that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s actions). Thus, we decline to remand proceedings on this basis. Accordingly, the following orders wilt be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
Roger Pauley
*3 FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent titles his brief “Respondent’s Brief in Support of Appeal” (Resp. Br. at 1). However, the respondent’s brief does not allege that the Immigration Judge erred in her decision, and only requests remand on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. As the respondent has presented previously unavailable evidence and requests a remand, we will construe the arguments presented in this brief as a motion to remand. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(iv), 1003.2(c)(4); see Matter of L-A-C-, 26 I&N Dec. 516 (BIA 2015).

2016 WL 6519985 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE GUERRERO-SOTO
File: AXXX XX5 150 - Houston, TX
September 22, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was previously before this Board on November 17, 2015, when the record of proceedings was remanded for adjudication of the respondent’s request for equitable tolling of the deadline for filing a motion to reopen. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 13, 2016, denying his motion to reopen. On appeal, the respondent contends that he exercised due diligence in pursuing his motion to reopen and that the Immigration Judge was impermissibly biased. The Department of Homeland Security did not file a brief on appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met their relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision that equitable tolling is not merited in this case to toll the deadline for filing the respondent’s motion to reopen. A “litigant is entitled to equitable tolling of a statute of limitations only if the litigant establishes two elements: (1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing.” Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, -- F.3d --, 2016 WL 4056051, at *5 (5th Cir. July 28, 2016). Even if the respondent had appealed his initial removal order and had not prevailed, he has not demonstrated his diligence and an extraordinary circumstance following the issuance of Garcia-Carias v. Holder, 697 F.3d 257 (5th Cir. 2012), which is the basis for his motion to reopen.
 
The Immigration Judge found without clear error that the respondent’s current counsel filed a Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28) on behalf of the respondent on October 22, 2012, which was after the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit issued Garcia-Carias, supra, on September 27, 2012. The respondent does not contend that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, and he has not submitted evidence to establish the nature of his attorney’s representation before the Immigration Court beginning in 2012, or otherwise demonstrated that he acted with “reasonable diligence” and that an “““‘extraordinary circumstance’ ‘beyond his control’ prevented him from complying with the applicable deadline.” See Lugo-Resendez, supra; see also Matter of S-M-, 22 I&N Dec. 49, 51 (BIA 1998) (noting that “statements in a brief, motion, or Notice of Appeal are not evidence and thus are not entitled to any evidentiary weight”). Upon review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not demonstrate that equitable tolling applies, as he has not shown due diligence in pursuing his claim.
 
*2 Moreover, we find no merit in the respondent’s contentions that the Immigration Judge was biased or acted other than impartially in conducting these removal proceedings. See Liteky v. United States, 510 U.S. 540, 554-56 (1994) (holding that “opinions formed by the judge on the basis of facts introduced or events occurring in the course of the current proceedings, or of prior proceedings, do not constitute a basis for a bias or partiality motion unless they display a deep seated favoritism or antagonism that would make fair judgment impossible”); see also Matter of Exame, 18 I&N Dec. 303, 306 (BIA 1982) (holding that an alien generally must show “personal, rather than judicial bias stemming from an ‘extrajudicial source’ which resulted in an opinion on the merits on some basis other than what the immigration judge learned from his participation in the case”).
 
The nature of the respondent’s second removal order was potentially relevant to the issue of whether he exercised due diligence in 2012 and, as the movant, it was the respondent’s burden to provide evidence in support of his motion. See section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). There is also no indication that the Immigration Judge’s criticism of the respondent’s counsel had any effect on the outcome of the case. Ultimately, the Immigration Judge’s conduct of these proceedings was fundamentally fair and does not reflect “bullying or hostile” treatment of the respondent’s counsel or the respondent.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 6519985 (BIA)

2016 WL 6392643 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DAVID ENRIQUE TREJO-JIMENEZ
File: AXXX XX9 624 - Harlingen, Texas
September 13, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Melinda M. Basaran, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s November 24, 2015, decision which denied his motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgment and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C).
 
The respondent was personally served on June 18, 2001, with the Notice to Appear, which bears his signature (Exh. 1). It informed him of his responsibility to report any change of address to immigration authorities, and the consequences for failure to appear were explained to him in Spanish. On July 16, 2001, a hearing notice for a hearing scheduled on October 5, 2001, was mailed to the respondent’s attorney and it was not returned by the Postal Service.
 
Following a hearing conducted in absentia on October 5, 2001, at which the respondent failed to appear and the Department of Homeland Security presented evidence, the Immigration Judge found the respondent subject to removal as charged, and ordered him removed from the United States. See generally section 242B of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b. A copy of the in absentia removal order was mailed to the respondent’s attorney, and it was not returned by the Postal Service.
 
On October 19, 2015, which was 14 years after he had been ordered removed, the respondent, through counsel, filed a motion to rescind his in absentia removal order because he claimed that his attorney had not informed him about the October 5, 2001, hearing. He alleged that he had been beaten by gang members in El Salvador after he refused their recruitment efforts, he requested reopening based on changed country conditions in the form of increased violence related to gangs and drug cartels, and he indicated that he fears criminal gang violence in El Salvador.
 
*2 We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s November 24, 2015, decision. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872 (BIA 1994). The respondent has not established that his absence at the scheduled hearing was the result of a lack of notice because his attorney received proper notice of the hearing (I.J. at 2). Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (notice to alien’s counsel constitutes notice to alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). He conceded that he did not have a “stable address” after he entered the United States, and that he “traveled from place to place.” However, he does not allege that he submitted change of address forms as required, and the record does not contain any change of address forms. To the extent that he claims that his attorney did not advise him about his hearing, he has not indicated that he informed her of his new address after he moved which would enable her to notify him about his hearing. If an alien does not fulfill his obligation to report his address change, notice of the hearing is not required pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(B), and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). See also Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354, 360-61 (5th Cir. 2009).
 
Furthermore, the respondent has not alleged or established that his prior attorney committed ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996) (alien seeking to reopen in absentia proceedings based upon her unsuccessful communications with attorney about hearing date did not establish exceptional circumstances where she failed to satisfy all of requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
In addition, the respondent has not alleged that he did not receive personal service of the Notice to Appear or that the consequences for failure to appear were not explained to him in Spanish. He has not indicated what actions, if any, he initiated to determine his immigration status after signing the Notice to Appear in 2001.
 
Finally, the respondent requested reopening to apply for asylum based upon the alleged existence of changed country conditions under section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(ii). Time limits do not apply to a motion to reopen to apply for asylum or withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in the country of nationality if such evidence is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the previous hearing. See Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247 (BIA 2007); section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). The respondent must meet a heavy burden of proof to demonstrate that if the proceedings were reopened with all the attendant delays, the new evidence offered would likely change the result in the case. Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161, 169 (BIA 2013); see also Matter of S-Y-G-, supra; Matter of C-W-L-, 24 I&N Dec. 346 (BIA 2007).
 
*3 The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had not established changed country conditions to qualify for reopening (I.J. at 2). We agree that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely, that the evidence submitted by the respondent in support of the motion did not establish that he qualified for any of the exceptions on the time restrictions, and that, even if his evidence established at most some worsening of conditions, it does not establish a material change in country conditions sufficient to meet the heavy burden to merit reopening inasmuch as gang violence and drug cartels have been prevalent in El Salvador for many years, Panjwani v. Gonzales, 401 F.3d 626, 633 (5th Cir. 2005) (violence between Muslims and Hindus was not uncommon in India at time of original hearing and evidence submitted with motion only indicated continuation of such violence); Matter of S-Y-G-, supra. To the extent that the respondent fears violence in El Salvador, these conditions affect the entire population of the country, and, without more, do not qualify an alien for asylum or withholding of removal in the United States. See, e.g., Matter of Sosa Ventura, 25 I&N Dec. 391, 394 (BIA 2010) (aliens fleeing general violence are not entitled to asylum or withholding of removal).
 
Moreover, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not merit sua sponte reopening under Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (I.J. at 3). We agree that he has not established that his case meets the standard of “““exceptional situations” for sua sponte reopening. Matter of J-J-, supra.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 6392643 (BIA)

2016 WL 6392652 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROSSANNA NUNEZ A.K.A. ROSSANNA CASTILLO
File: AXXX XX3 671 - New York, NY
September 9, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alison Berry, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 20, 2015, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider the Immigration Judge’s November 13, 2014, decision, in which she denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider because the motion did not specify any error of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s decision denying her motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). In her motion to reconsider, the respondent asserted that the Immigration Judge had overlooked a question of fact in her motion to reopen, specifically that she had alleged that she had received ineffective assistance of counsel from her previous representative. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reconsider because she concluded that she had not overlooked an ineffective assistance claim in the respondent’s motion to reopen but rather that the respondent had not made such a claim in her motion to reopen.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent had not set forth a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in her motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion to reopen states that she had “reconsidered her decision to agree to an Order of Voluntary Departure,1 believes that her previous decision was ill-advised” and that she was “not previously aware of the legal ramifications of being grandfathered under her step-father’s petition.” In her affidavit in support of her motion to reopen, the respondent stated that it was “possible that [she] did not receive the proper legal advice.” These statements are insufficient to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, particularly in the absence of any indication of substantial compliance with the procedures set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46-47 (2d Cir. 2005). We recognize that the respondent has since taken steps to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, but this does not alter our conclusion that the Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent’s motion to reconsider does not identify any error in her previous decision denying the motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2) (a motion to reconsider shall specify “errors of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority”); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Accordingly, we likewise affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reconsider.
 
*2 We acknowledge the respondent’s contention on appeal that her case should be deferred pending the possible implementation of the Deferred Action for Parents of Americans and Lawful Permanent Residents (“DAPA”) program. A request for prosecutorial discretion lies within the exclusive jurisdiction of the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

As the Immigration Judge noted in her November 13, 2014, decision, because the respondent filed a motion to reopen during the voluntary departure period granted pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b), the grant of voluntary departure is terminated automatically. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(iii). The respondent has not challenged this conclusion on appeal.

2016 WL 6392672 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAQUEL LUZIA FERREIRA PEREIRA
File: AXXX XX2 663 - Boston, MA
September 1, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vard R. Johnson, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 14, 2015, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen her removal proceedings conducted in absentia on January 5, 2010.1 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) did not file an opposition to the motion or to this appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The record reflects that a Notice to Appear (NTA) was served on the respondent by regular mail on September 30, 2009, to the address2 that she provided on her application for adjustment of status based on her marriage to a United States citizen (Form I-485), dated October 22, 2004, and the NTA was not returned by the postal service (I.J. at 1, 3; Exhs. 1, 2). The respondent was subsequently sent a notice of hearing (NOH) by regular mail to that same address on December 18, 2009, informing the respondent of a January 5, 2010, hearing date, and that notice was returned by the postal service (I.J. at 1). The respondent did not appear for her hearing, and on January 5, 2010, an in absentia order of removal was entered and mailed to the respondent via regular mail, again to the same address, and it was returned by the postal service (I.J. at 1). On June 24, 2014, the respondent, through counsel Micol Mion Gordon, Esquire, filed a motion to reopen in which the respondent alleged that she was denied due process because the NTA was issued years after her Form I-485 was denied.
 
In his December 14, 2015, decision, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding that the respondent failed to rebut the presumption that the NTA was delivered to her last known address at 9 Marney Street. In particular, the Immigration Judge noted that the NTA had not been returned by the postal service, and while the NOH had been returned by the postal service, the respondent had provided no evidence to support her assertion that she had moved to a new address.
 
We first observe that the respondent has a legal obligation to provide an address at which she can be contacted. See section 239(a)(1)(F)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1)(F)(i). In addition, we have held that an alien can be charged with receiving proper notice when notice “reaches the correct address but does not reach the alien through some failure in the internal workings of the household.” Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181, 189 (BIA 2001).
 
*2 We presume that a properly addressed notice sent via regular mail is received by the addressee. See Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008). The Board considers all relevant evidence in determining whether an alien has rebutted the presumption. Such includes but is not limited to factors such as affidavits from the respondent and others who are knowledgeable about whether notice was received, whether due diligence was exercised in seeking to redress the situation, any prior applications for relief that would indicate an incentive to appear, and the respondent’s prior appearance at immigration proceedings, if applicable. See also Shah v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 25 (1st Cir. 2008); Matter of C-R-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 677 (BIA 2008).
 
In the respondent’s motion to reopen of June 24, 2014, the respondent admitted in her motion that she received the DHS denial of her Application for Adjustment of Status (Form I-485) mailed on October 15, 2005, which had been mailed to her 9 Marney Street address, and she averred that she kept receiving mail at that address even after she physically moved out. The respondent alleged that it was a denial of her due process for a NTA to be issued over a year after the Form I-485 had been denied. The respondent further alleged that she did not discover her in absentia order until she hired counsel apparently recently in furtherance of a new Form I-485 filing. No affidavit or other evidence accompanied the motion.
 
On appeal, the respondent now files a new motion to reopen predicated upon ineffective assistance of counsel (IAC) by her prior counsel, Ms. Gordon. The respondent has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent alleges that Ms. Gordon committed IAC by failing to provide the respondent’s affidavit and other evidence with the June 24, 2014, motion to reopen.
 
The respondent has proffered an affidavit, dated February 3, 2016, in which she alleges that she did not receive the NTA or the NOH, and she further alleges that she moved from 9 Marney Street in April 2005. The respondent also lists the addresses to which she purportedly moved since then. The respondent avers that she received the October 15, 2005, DHS denial of her Form I-485 mailed to the 9 Marney Street address because although she was no longer living there, a “resident at that address called” and the respondent picked it up from that resident.
 
The respondent has also proffered a statement from her prior counsel, Ms. Gordon, in response to the IAC allegations. In Ms. Gordon’s statement, she asserts that she determined that it was unwise and unethical to submit an affidavit from the respondent given her inability to conclusively tell counsel where she lived and when. Ms. Gordon also provided notes showing the list of addresses and dates provided by the respondent and they are at least in part inconsistent with the dates and addresses the respondent now provides in her IAC claim. Ms. Gordon further notes that she contacted the respondent’s friend, Cristina Lira, who continued to reside at 9 Marney Street, and Ms. Lira was unable to confirm whether she had received the NTA, and thus Ms. Gordon determined that she could not submit an affidavit from Ms. Lira. The respondent has proffered an affidavit, dated March 9, 2016, in rebuttal to Ms. Gordon’s statement. The respondent avers that Ms. Gordon never told the respondent that her information was unreliable or that evidence in support of the motion was needed.
 
*3 We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the motion to reopen, for the reasons stated therein. Most significantly, here the Immigration Judge considered the respondent’s evidence with the motion to reopen and found that the respondent had failed to rebut the presumption of proper delivery under Matter of M-R-A-, supra. The respondent has not demonstrated that she had an incentive to appear, where her application for adjustment of status had been previously denied, or that she exercised due diligence in inquiring into her immigration status. See also Matter of C-R-C-, supra (holding that the respondent overcame the presumption of delivery of a Notice to Appear sent by regular mail where he submitted an affidavit stating that he did not receive the notice, additional evidence indicating he had an incentive to appear, and by exercising due diligence in promptly obtaining counsel and requesting reopening of the proceedings).
 
We will also deny the motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of former counsel. The respondent has not established deficient performance and further has failed to establish that she was prejudiced by Ms. Gordon’s representation. See Muyubisnay-Cungachi v. Holder, 734 F.3d 66 (1st Cir. 2013) (an alien bringing an ineffective assistance claim based on counsel’s performance during removal proceedings must establish both a deficient performance by counsel and a reasonable probability of prejudice resulting from his former representation); Zeru v. Gonzales, 503 F.3d 59, 72 (1st Cir. 2007) (stating that “to succeed on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, petitioners must show ‘a reasonable probability of prejudice’ resulting from their former representation”).
 
First, the respondent’s counsel informs that the Office of Bar Counsel has determined that it would not go forward with the complaint against Ms. Gordon as the asserted ethical violations did not rise to the magnitude it requires for a full grievance and hearing proceeding. Second, Ms. Gordon credibly explains her actions regarding the motion to reopen she filed, and her strategic decision not to include an affidavit from the respondent or her friend. Indeed, counsel’s strategic decision in this regard is not the type of conduct typically held to be “ineffective assistance.” See Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113, 127 n. 7 (BIA 1998) (citing Thorsteinsson v. INS, 724 F.2d 1365 (9th Cir. 1984)) (ineffective assistance counsel claim rejected where attorney made informed, strategic decision). Third, Ms. Gordon’s statement and her notes attached establish inconsistencies in the respondent’s assertions as to where she resided since 2001. Fourth, the respondent has not provided a statement from her friend who continued to reside at 9 Marney Street, to corroborate that she did not receive the NTA; nor has she provided objective corroboration for the addresses at which she claims to have resided, with the exception of the 68 Linden Street address. Consequently, the respondent has not met her burden to overcome the presumption of delivery of the NTA.
 
*4 The formal rescission requirements need not be satisfied in certain circumstances where the respondent is eligible for relief that was unavailable at the time of the in absentia hearing and did not receive oral warnings in his/her native language regarding the bar to future discretionary relief that arises from a failure to appear. See generally Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). In such cases, the motion to reopen is subject to the 90 day filing deadline set forth at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). The respondent’s in absentia removal order was entered on January 5, 2010. The June 24, 2014, motion was thus long past the 90 day deadline for the filing of a motion to reopen seeking new relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b).
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that no exceptional circumstances have been presented to warrant sua sponte reopening. Pursuant to our holding in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), the power to reopen sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

See Decision of the Board of Immigration Appeals dated September 2, 2015. Our review now includes the most recent decision issued by the Immigration Judge on December 14, 2015.

2

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE LUIS CRUZ-CRUZ
File: AXXX XX2 292 - Chicago, IL
August 31, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tabitha Villarrubia, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Elizabeth Treacy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled (conceded)
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 18, 2014, decision denying his motion for a continuance and ordering him removed. He also requests remand of the record for the Immigration Judge to further consider his request for a continuance of these proceedings based on new evidence. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed; and the motion to remand, denied.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility findings, for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); see also Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 2007); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review questions of law, discretion, or judgment, and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for additional time to investigate possible eligibility for a derivative U nonimmigrant visa (Respondent’s Brief at 4-7; Respondent’s December 18, 2014, Motion to Continue at 2-3). See generally section 101(a)(15)(U)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(15)(U)(ii) (allowing certain relatives, including spouses, of the victims of qualifying crimes to establish eligibility for U nonimmigrant status as derivative beneficiaries).
 
The Immigration Judge has broad discretion to grant a continuance for good cause. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. We will not overturn an Immigration Judge’s decision to deny a continuance unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing. Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987). We consider the following factors when addressing continuances sought to apply for a U visa: (1) the DHS’s response to the continuance motion; (2) whether the underlying visa petition is prima facie approvable; and (3) the reason for the continuance and other procedural factors. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807, 812-813 (BIA 2012). Here, the DHS opposed the respondent’s motion to continue before the Immigration Judge and reaffirms the opposition on appeal (DHS Brief at 6-8; Tr. at 13-14). We, therefore, consider the remaining factors as outlined in Matter of Sanchez Sosa, supra, at 812-13.
 
*2 First, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that, based on the record as constituted before her, the respondent did not show his prima facie eligibility for derivative U visa status (I.J. at 3-4). The respondent was not then married to the potential principal U visa applicant (I.J. at 3-4; Respondent’s Motion to Continue at 3). See section 101(a)(15)(U)(ii)(II) of the Act. When coupled with the fact that the respondent’s fiancée had not yet filed an application for a U visa or obtained a signed law enforcement certificate (“LEC”), the Immigration Judge reasonably concluded that the respondent’s potential U visa eligibility was speculative (I.J. at 3; Respondent’s Motion to Continue at 2-3). See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(2)(i) (noting that an applicant for a U visa must submit a signed LEC attesting that the applicant has been a victim of a qualifying crime and has been, is being, or is likely to be helpful in the investigation or prosecution of the crime); see also Matter of Sanchez Sosa, supra, at 811.
 
Second, we are unpersuaded by the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge did not properly consider, when denying his continuance request, that he had obtained new counsel approximately two weeks prior to his final hearing (Respondent’s Brief at 5, 7; I.J. at 2-3; Respondent’s Motion to Continue at 2). The Immigration Judge acknowledged that the respondent had new counsel, and nonetheless found further continuances unwarranted (I.J. at 2-4). Specifically, the Immigration Judge considered the length of the respondent’s removal proceedings, the fact that he had been represented by counsel at all of his hearings, and the 18-month continuance granted at the previous hearing for the respondent to prepare any forms of relief for which he may have been eligible (I.J. at 2-4; Tr. at 9, 11).
 
Further, the respondent argues the Immigration Judge did not consider that his identification documents were stolen, and thus he could not marry his fiancée prior to the scheduled hearing because he was awaiting replacements (Respondent’s Brief at 5; I.J. at 3-4; Respondent’s Motion to Continue at 3). Notwithstanding the respondent’s argument, we are unpersuaded by his assertion that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s continuance denial (Respondent’s Brief at 3; I.J. at 3-4). See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-57 (BIA 1983) (“[A]n immigration judge’s decision denying the motion for continuance will not be reversed unless the alien establishes that that denial caused him actual prejudice and harm and materially affected the outcome of his case.”); see also Matter of Perez-Andrade, supra, at 434. Notably, as outlined above, even setting aside that he and his fiancée had not yet married, the respondent did not otherwise establish prima facie eligibility for derivative U visa classification.
 
*3 Based on the foregoing, we conclude that the respondent was not denied a full and fair hearing.1 See Matter of Perez-Andrade, supra, at 434. We, therefore, affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to continue.
 
The respondent also seeks remand based on new evidence that he is now married and his wife is the beneficiary of a signed LEC (Respondent’s Brief at 7, Tab A).2 Notably, notwithstanding the passage of time, the respondent has not otherwise presented evidence establishing his or his wife’s prima facie eligibility for U visa classification. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, supra, at 811, 814-15 (explaining that (1) the principal applicant has the burden to establish prima facie eligibility; (2) any U visa applicant must establish that he is admissible or that he has sought and qualifies for any necessary waivers of inadmissibility; and (3) as appropriate, the applicant should generally provide the Immigration Judge with any completed U visa applications and filing receipts issued by United States Citizenship and Immigration Services to demonstrate that he is prosecuting his U visa application and is prima facie eligible for this benefit); 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(b), (c)(2), (c)(4). Because the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for a U visa, we will deny his request for remand. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-72 (BIA 1992) (holding that the Board may deny a motion to remand where prima facie eligibility for relief is not established); see also 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(b) (discussing requirements for a U visa).3
 
We will not address the respondent’s argument on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his request for voluntary departure, as the respondent specifically declined to seek this relief at his final hearing (Respondent’s Brief at 3; Tr. at 14-15). See Matter of D-R-, 25 I&N Dec. 445, 457 (BIA 2011); Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260, 261 n.1 (BIA 2007). Similarly, although the respondent generally maintains he is eligible for cancellation of removal for certain non-permanent residents under section 240A(b), 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), we note that he has not submitted any persuasive evidence, or even made a proffer, to demonstrate prima facie eligibility for this benefit (Notice of Appeal). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*4 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the respondent alleged on the Notice of Appeal that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, he did not pursue this argument in his brief nor did he comply with the Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), requirements (Notice of Appeal From a Decision of an Immigration Judge (Form EOIR-26)). See Guzman-Rivadeneira v. Lynch, 822 F.3d 978, 983-84 (7th Cir. 2016) (requiring compliance with the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, as necessary in showing ineffective assistance of counsel). Therefore, we will not address this issue further. See Matter of G-G-S-, 26 I&N Dec. 339, 340 n.2 (BIA 2014).

See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (limiting our appellate fact-finding authority to “taking administrative notice of commonly known facts such as . . . the contents of official documents”); Matter of S-H-, supra, at 465-66.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOJIDDIN BERDIEV
File: AXXX XX7 737 - Denver, CO
August 30, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Youry Ziankovich, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final administrative order in this matter was entered by the Board on February 24, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal,1 and reinstated his 60-day period of voluntary departure that was granted by the Immigration Judge. On May 19, 2016, he filed a motion to reopen asserting eligibility for adjustment of status based on the visa petition filed by his current United States citizen wife. It is also alleged that the respondent received ineffective assistance from former counsel in his proceedings before the Immigration Judge and in conjunction with his Board appeal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely because it was filed more than 90 days after we rendered the final administrative decision in his case on February 24, 2015. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). He also has not demonstrated that his motion falls within any exception to the 90-day filing deadline, as provided for under the statute and regulations. See section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i)-(iv).
 
In moving to reopen, it is asserted that the respondent received ineffective assistance by former counsel who, it is said, did not timely notify him that his Board appeal had been dismissed, and that his 60-day period of voluntary departure had been reinstated. Motion to Reopen (Motion) at #4, #15-16, #22-25. However, his submissions do not demonstrate satisfaction with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.2
 
We also decline to reopen the respondent’s removal proceedings sua sponte, as there has been no showing of exceptional circumstances warranting such action. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999). Becoming eligible for adjustment of status after entry of an alien’s final administrative decision does not, in itself, constitute an exceptional circumstance warranting reopening on our own motion. Moreover, the respondent has not shown that the visa petition filed by his current USC wife has been approved. Thus, he has not demonstrated current prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status.3
 
*2 It is also claimed that former counsel provided ineffective assistance by not pursuing an asylum application for the respondent at his removal hearing, and that this constitutes exceptional circumstances justifying sua sponte reopening. Motion at #28-32. This contention has been advanced by current counsel, not the respondent himself. It is well settled that statements by counsel do not constitute evidence. Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980); Matter of Laureano; 19 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (BIA 1983), n. 2. In any event, as noted above, there has been no showing of compliance with the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance by former counsel in accordance with Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
We further observe that the respondent has not requested or demonstrated eligibility for reopening to apply for asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions/circumstances arising in Tajikistan since his last hearing that materially affect his current eligibility for such relief. Section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii).
 
Finally, we deny the request to “rescind” the respondent’s order of removal. Motion at #1. The respondent does not deny that he is removable as charged, as found by the Immigration Judge. He also has not demonstrated eligibility for relief from removal.
 
Insofar as the respondent seeks a favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion, or a stay of removal based on his personal circumstances, he must direct his requests to the DHS.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Hope Malia Holiona
FOR THE BOARD

In addition, he has not cited any controlling circuit law establishing that the United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit applies the doctrine of equitable tolling in cases such as this. In any event, he has not demonstrated compliance with Matter of Lozada, id., regarding claims of ineffective assistance by attorney Brett Davies, who last represented him at his hearing and on appeal.

2016 WL 6392708 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUBEN DARIO RODRIGUEZ-GARCIA A.K.A. RAUL MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX4 661 - Omaha, NE
August 26, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James A. Benzoni, Esquire
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. He appeals from an Immigration Judge’s July 21, 2015, summary order, which stated that he had waived the right to appeal. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed, based on lack of jurisdiction, as the respondent waived the right to appeal to the Board.
 
The Immigration Judge granted a period of pre-hearing voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), and. noted that the respondent had waived the right to appeal to the Board. Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000) (grant of voluntary departure under section 240B(a) of the Act requires a waiver of the right to appeal); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(D). The transcript of proceedings shows that the respondent, represented by counsel, waived appeal after assuring the Immigration Judge that he had explained to the respondent that a waiver meant that the order would be final (Tr. at 10-11). On August 17, 2015, the respondent nevertheless filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26).1
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(G) (summary dismissal of appeal appropriate where appeal is barred based on waiver of right to appeal).
 
The respondent fails to show that the waiver was invalid. As noted, the respondent was represented by counsel at the final hearing on July 21, 2015. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, supra, at 1322-23.
 
The respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, and therefore did not knowingly waive the right to appeal to the Board. However, he fails to show prejudice resulting from the claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. Valencia v. Holder, 657 F.3d 745, 749 (8th Cir. 2011), citing Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988). The respondent has not demonstrated that he is prima facie eligible for any relief available to him in removal proceedings.
 
*2 At the July 21, 2015, removal hearing, counsel conceded that the respondent was ineligible for any relief in removal proceedings and was continuing to prepare his U non-immigrant visa application (Tr. at 9). Counsel for the respondent therefore pursued a grant of voluntary departure, and waived appeal (Tr. at 9-11).
 
The respondent’s brief seeks to allege ineffective assistance of counsel with regards to the respondent’s application for a U non-immigrant visa, but does not address any error in his prior counsel’s concession that the respondent’s only eligible relief in removal proceedings is voluntary departure.
 
Applicable regulations pertaining to U-visas state clearly that “USCIS has sole jurisdiction over all petitions for U nonimmigrant status.” 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1). In addition, with regard to aliens subject to a final order of removal, the regulations make clear that an alien with an outstanding removal order may nevertheless pursue a petition for a U-visa with the USCIS and that the alien “may file a request for a stay of removal” with the USCIS pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a) and 8 C.F.R. § 1241.6(a). 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1)(ii). Neither the Board nor the Immigration Judge has jurisdiction over a U-visa petition. Thus, the respondent has not established prejudice from prior counsel’s actions or inactions.
 
The appeal will, therefore, be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction, and the record of proceedings is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent subsequently filed a motion to remand with the Board on October 29, 2016. However, since we lack jurisdiction over the respondent’s appeal, we lack jurisdiction over his motion to remand.

2016 WL 6137155 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIGUEL ANGEL MOLINA
File: AXXX XX9 431 - New York, NY
August 25, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anthony M. Giordano, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Fen Lu
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on February 20, 2014, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. On May 13, 2016, the respondent filed another motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred. Section 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent, however, argues that his proceedings should be reopened based on the ineffective assistance by attorney George Echevarria, who represented him before the Immigration Judge, on appeal, and in his prior motion to reopen. The respondent also argues that the motion should be granted because the form I-130 visa petition filed by his lawful permanent resident spouse was approved by the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services in February 2016. We will address these arguments in turn.
 
First, the respondent argues that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance. However, the respondent has not shown that he has sufficiently complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent’s affidavit submitted in support of the motion states that the former counsel “has been provided with a copy of this affidavit and has not responded to date” (Motion Exh. H, at ¶ 5). The respondent, however, has not explained how former counsel was provided a copy of “this affidavit,” which states that former counsel was provided with a copy of the document and has not responded. The respondent also has not presented other documentary evidence showing that his statement regarding ineffective assistance of counsel claim was provided to the former counsel, or when.
 
Furthermore, the respondent has not explained how he was prejudiced by the former counsel’s performance. Although former counsel appears to have continued to represent the respondent until at least February 2016 (see Motion Exh. E), the respondent has not explained how his recent performance was inadequate or how he was prejudiced by such ineffective assistance. The respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel appears to be related only to the denial of a continuance by the Immigration Judge at his October 31, 2011, hearing date. The respondent has not explained why he did not file an ineffective assistance of counsel claim earlier regarding the alleged deficient performance in 2011. See, e.g., Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127 (2d Cir. 2008) (discussing due diligence in filing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
*2 The respondent’s statement submitted with the motion states that on October 31, 2011, the day of his previous hearing, the former counsel “was not ready to go forward and as a result the [Immigration] Court decided to order me removed” and, although the former counsel filed a motion to continue the case and provided proof of mailing the motion, “the [Immigration] Court indicated that it did not receive the motion and also that other papers were not filed by the deadline set by the [Immigration] Court” (Motion Exh. H, at ¶¶ 3-4; see Tr. at 37). However, the respondent states that he decided not to file a bar complaint against the former counsel because he “made an innocent mistake calendaring the matter” (Motion Exh. H, at ¶ 6). The respondent further admits that, at the October 31, 2011, hearing date, he had not provided supporting documents for his cancellation of removal application that had been requested by the former counsel, and that would have been “critical” to the success of the respondent’s case (id.). The respondent, therefore, has not made “a prima facie showing that he would have been eligible for the relief and that he could have made a strong showing in support of his application,” such that he was prejudiced by any ineffective assistance of counsel.1 Rabiu v. INS, 41 F.3d 879, 882 (2d Cir. 1994). Based on the above, the respondent has not shown that his proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of the former counsel.
 
We will next address the respondent’s arguments based on the approval of a Form i-130 visa petition filed by his lawful permanent resident spouse and seeking adjustment of status. This aspect of the motion does not fall within any exception to the motion to reopen time limitations enumerated in section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). The respondent urges us to sua sponte reopen his proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), based on the circumstances of his case, including the claim of former counsel’s ineffective assistance, the USCIS delay in adjudicating the visa petition, and other factors. We do not find that the respondent’s case presents an exceptional situation that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the power to reopen sua sponte “is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”).
 
Based on the above, the respondent’s motion will be denied. The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
*3 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

The record also shows that there was concern about the respondent’s eligibility for cancellation of removal, in light of his criminal record (I.J. at 3-4; Tr. at 40-41). The respondent’s motion does not address the issue of whether he would have been statutorily eligible for cancellation of removal on October 31, 2011. The respondent’s statement only suggests that a sex offense charge in 1997 has been dismissed but this was not reflected in the documentary evidence available in 2011 (Motion Exh. H, at ¶ 6.c).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JULBERT GALINDO-ONOFRE A.K.A. JULBERT ONOFRE GALINDO
File: AXXX XX6 240 - San Diego, CA
August 25, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alan Michael Anzarouth, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred. The Immigration Judge entered the final administrative order in this case on October 22, 2014, when he granted the respondent pre-conclusion voluntary departure in lieu of a removal order. A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, and only one may be filed. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) & (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent filed the present motion on May 10, 2016, over 1 year after the Immigration Judge’s final decision, and a prior motion to reopen was denied by the Immigration Judge on December 19, 2014. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent urges that the time and number limits should be equitably tolled with regard to his motion because he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The time and number limits for motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where an alien raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). An alien must, however, demonstrate that he or she has been prejudiced by counsel’s conduct. Id.; Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999) (finding that prejudice must be shown for a due process challenge and that prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected outcome); see also Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004) (an alien can show prejudice where counsel’s performance “may have affected the outcome of the proceedings”). The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
In this case, the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective representation of his prior counsels. The respondent has not supported his motion with any applications for relief, including an asylum application as required by regulation nor has he articulated a basis for such relief. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1), (3)(ii). Thus, we are not persuaded that the respondent’s alleged fear of harm in Mexico is adequately supported. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104 (1988) (a motion to reopen may be denied if alien does not establish prima facie case for the relief sought); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (to prevail on a motion to reopen an alien must establish that “the new evidence offered would likely change the result in the case”). Further, statements of counsel in a brief or motion are not evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Consequently, the respondent has not demonstrated that the outcome might have been affected by his prior counsels. In addition, given this lack of evidence, the respondent has not shown that an “exceptional situation” exists that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

2016 WL 6392713 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AUGUSTINE BOATENG
File: AXXX XX0 886 - Cleveland, OH
August 25, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rahul Dev Manchanda, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ghana, appeals from the February 11, 2016, decision of the Immigration Judge denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear (Form I-862) on December 14, 1999, and was ordered removed after a hearing held in absentia on May 11, 2001 (Exhs. 1K, 2K). He filed a motion to reopen on September 11, 2015, arguing that he was previously subject to ineffective assistance of counsel. We point out, as did the Immigration Judge, that the respondent states that he met the attorney in question in 2006, many years after the in absentia order was issued (I.J. at 3). The Immigration Judge found that any alleged ineffective assistance in 2006 has no bearing on the legitimacy of the order of removal entered in absentia in 2001 (I.J. at 3). The respondent has not addressed the Immigration Judge’s finding in this regard.
 
In general, a motion to reopen is required to be filed within 90 days of a final decision. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). To rescind an order of removal entered in absentia, a respondent must file a motion within 180 days demonstrating that his failure to appear at the hearing was “because of exceptional circumstances.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); see also Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). The motion may be filed at any time, if the respondent demonstrates that he did not receive proper notice of the hearing. Id. The respondent, here, has made no claim that he did not receive notice nor has he presented any claim to exceptional circumstances that prevented him from appearing in 2001. As noted above, the respondent claims that his attorney from 2006 was ineffective, but does not address his failure to appear in 2001. See Exhs. 1K, 2K.
 
The respondent requests that the proceedings be reopened to allow him to establish eligibility for adjustment of status, and asylum and related relief and protection. As observed by the Immigration Judge, although a visa petition has apparently been filed on his behalf, there is no indication that it has been approved (I.J. at 4). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status, nor would newly available relief be a basis to reopen proceedings. Moreover, as the respondent seeks equitable tolling to remedy the untimeliness of his motion, the respondent has not met the requirement of establishing due diligence. See Mezo v. Holder, 615 F.3d 616, 620 (6th Cir. 2010); Tapia-Matinez v. Gonzales, 482 F.3d 417, 422-24 (6th Cir. 2007). The respondent was ordered removed in 2001, married in 2004, and has presented no reason for not contacting an attorney regarding his case until 2006. More significantly, however, is that even if his attorney in 2006 was ineffective, the respondent did not contact his current attorney until 2015. He has presented no reasons for this delay.
 
*2 A motion to reopen to apply for asylum and withholding of removal is not subject to the filing deadline if it is based on changed conditions arising within the country to which deportation has been ordered. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i); Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013). However, the evidence must be new and material and not have been available for presentation at the previous hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). Also, the motion must be filed within a reasonable period after the changed circumstances. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii); Matter of T-M-H- & S-W-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 193 (BIA 2010).
 
The respondent has not met this burden. As observed by the Immigration Judge, it does not appear the respondent submitted an asylum application with his motion to reopen (I.J. at 3). The record contains the respondent’s submission, numbered pages 1 through 95, but there is no asylum application. He has submitted an asylum application on appeal. However, the brief comments on the application concerning the government of Ghana, the respondent’s fear of Muslim extremists, and his claim that he knows some people who were killed, with no supporting evidence, does not establish how country conditions have materially changed since his hearing, or that he could establish a prima facie case of eligibility for asylum. The respondent has not met his heavy burden of showing reopening is warranted. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314(1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
Finally, there is no merit to the respondent’s claim that he has presented extraordinary circumstances that support our reopening the proceedings sua sponte. Sua sponte reopening is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations.” Rais v. Holder, 768 F.3d 453, 460 (6th Cir. 2014) (sua sponte authority is available only “in exceptional situations”); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1137 (BIA 1999) (an alien’s ability to become eligible for adjustment of status due to the passage of time after a failure to voluntarily depart is not an exceptional situation); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also INS v. Doherty, supra (motions to reopen are especially disfavored in immigration proceedings because every delay works to the advantage of the deportable alien). The respondent was ordered removed in 2001, failed to appear for his hearing, and remained in this country illegally. The fact that he has equities in this country, including his family, and may potentially be eligible for relief at some point, does not present such circumstances. See Matter of G-D-, supra.
 
*3 Based on the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2016 WL 6392713 (BIA)

2016 WL 6137142 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALBERTANO GUZMAN-ALBINO
File: AXXX XX8 398 - Chicago, IL
August 24, 2016
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brian A. Seyfried, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michelle M. Venci
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on November 10, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On July 13, 2016, more than VA years after the Board’s order, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent, however, argues that his proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Jorge A. Rodriguez, his former counsel who represented him at hearing and on appeal.
 
We will first address the respondent’s argument that he should be deemed to have satisfied the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), because the former counsel was suspended from the practice of law. The respondent argues that, because the former counsel was already suspended, he could not contact the former counsel to inform him about the ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and it would be futile to file a bar complaint against him (Motion, at LA1). However, a document submitted with the motion regarding the former counsel, which appears to have been printed out on June 24, 2016, contains the former counsel’s contact information with his business address and telephone numbers (Motion Exh. C, at 8). Furthermore, although the former counsel was suspended from the practice of law for a specified period from October 16, 2013, to November 15, 2013, documents submitted by both the respondent and the DHS show that he is currently “active” and authorized to practice law (Motion Exh. C; DHS Opp. Attachment). Under these circumstances, the respondent’s explanations on why he should be deemed to have complied with Matter of Lozada, supra, are unfounded and insufficient.
 
In addition, the respondent’s motion is untimely as noted above, and the respondent has not shown how he has exercised due diligence since the Board’s final administrative decision before filing the motion to reopen. The United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit has required that, while a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel may be subject to equitable tolling of the motion time limitations, an alien must demonstrate “““due diligence” in order to succeed on a claim for equitable tolling. El-Gazawy v. Holder, 690 F.3d 852, 859 (7th Cir. 2012); Johnson v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 795, 799 (7th Cir. 2007); Patel v. Gonzales, 442 F.3d 1011, 1016 (7th Cir. 2006). The respondent’s motion prepared by the counsel suggests that the respondent was not aware of the final order of removal until he was taken into immigration custody, which occurred in 2016 (Motion, I.B). However, counsel’s statement in a motion or brief is not evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Furthermore, the respondent’s statement submitted with the motion states that in 2013 he was aware of the order of removal and appealed the decision, although the appeal was unsuccessful (Motion Exh. A, at ¶ 92). Therefore, the respondent has not shown that he was not aware of the order of removal until 2016, or that he has practiced due diligence.
 
*2 Even if the respondent’s motion were to be considered timely and the respondent had complied with Matter of Lozada, supra, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is unsupported by the record of his case. The respondent argues that the former counsel provided ineffective assistance by not telling him about his final hearing date at the Immigration Court, which was March 6, 2013 (Motion, I.A.1; Motion Exh. A, at ¶¶ 6-8, 11). However, the record of proceedings of this case shows that the respondent did attend his March 6, 2013, hearing with the former counsel (Tr. at 29). The record also shows that the respondent was orally advised of the Immigration Judge’s decision and his appeal rights through an interpreter (Tr. at 29, 39-40). Therefore, the respondent’s factual assertions regarding ineffective assistance of former counsel are unfounded and contradicted by the record of this case.3
 
In sum, the respondent has not shown that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance by not informing him of his hearing date at the Immigration Court. The respondent also has not shown that the motion to reopen time limitations should be equitably tolled in his case, or that contacting the former counsel or filing a bar complaint in compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, was impossible and/or futile. Based on the above, the respondent’s motion will be denied. The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

Even if were to assume that this statement was actually from the respondent and therefore constitutes “““evidence,” it still would not support the factual assertions in the motion as noted above.
